THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps equal
to the price stated, books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address:—
Contemporary Science.
The development of the theory of evolution, that grand conception which unites
all phenomena in one splendid synthesis under uniform law, regains the most attrac-
tive feature of contemporary science. From year to year the hypothesis is altered
in its details, but its general truth becomes always clearer, and those who now reject
it do so only by impugning the quality of their intellect. It is not as if there were
rival theories of defensible validity. All the facts point one way. For creation or
emanation there are assumptions and presumptions innumerable, but not a vestige
of ponderable proof. For evolution there is all the proof that is. Its chain of evi-
dence may not be complete ; the links that are wanting have probably been lost for
ever, but sufficient remain to amply justify the general judgment of scientists.
Sporadic objectors, like Lord Salisbury, doubtfully suggesting the argument for
design to the consideration of the British Association; or Mrs. Besant, confidently
compaiing the weakness of inductive logic with the sweet reasonableness sweated
from the swoons of a Master-Mahatma, are mere retrograde ripples swallowed in the
advancing tide of thought. Lord Salisbury’s cavil that physicists and biologists
cannot agree as to time available and time demanded for the processes of
evolution, gives ground for believing that either physicists or biologists, or
both, are in error; but affords no basis for postulating supernatural inter-
ference with observed uniformity of natural law. Mrs. Besant’s quotation of
Weismann as denying the perpetuation of acquired structural variations, and of
Huxley ms admitting that ethical advance is opposed to the scheme of natural
selection, is a pleasant irrelevancy of the platform. Neither Weismann nor Huxley,
as Mrs. Besant might add at future lectures, has the least idea that the evolu
tionary theory is weakened by their admissions. It is only modified. Weismann
simply shifts the load from natural selection to sexual selection; and Huxley, while
re-stating the tiuism that altruism is opposed to egoism, recognises as a matter of
course that both altruism and egoism join rhythmically in evolutionary unity.
The tendency of current thought is to show that the individual is in the plan
of existence as nothing compared with the race—that the most complex individual
lives are, in Fosters phrase, “but the bye-play of ovum-bearing organisms.” Here
again evolution comes directly into conflict with Oriental creeds which lay stress or.
the need for purification of the Ego by a short or long course of trial, with a view
to final salvation. More and more clearly is it to be seen that this view is un-
scientific and unwarrantable—the dear delusion of anthropocentric myths which
measure the Atom with the All, and hail him master of a universe where his function
is solely that of a life-bearer, and his identity is a matter of the profoundest cosmic
indifference.
In the “Contemporary Science Series,” a list of which appears below, may be
found the most recent and readable books on evolution, as on many other minor
phases of scientific struggle. Such works as Drummond’s “ Ascent of Man ” and
Kidds “Social Evolution,” written to bolster preconceived theories, cannot compare
in interest and value with a book like “ The Evolution of Sex,” a most masterly
review of a wide and fascinating field. Weismann’s “ Germ-Plasm ”is strong meat
for beginners, and wants some previous training to be appreciated. Lombroso’s
“Man of Genius” and Ellis’s “Criminal” and “Man and Woman” (elsewhere
noted) have special charm for average readers ; but the whole series can be warmly
recommended. Posted for 3s. 9d. each, with exceptions noted.

Greece fell. Her poets sang, her sculptors wrought
Divinely ; none had ever heard before
Such wondrous lessons as her sages taught—
Greece is no more.
Rome fell. From east to west, from north to
south,
Grandeur and glory followed where she led,
And conquered lands learned wisdom from her
mouth—
Great Rome is dead.
And England ? She of Nature’s charms possessed,
Her messages from pole to pole both send—

J.C.M.
The Australian adjective again. Parson
riding along bush-road, overtakes a horseman and
enters into conversation. After a bit, parson —
“How is it that a young fellow with some educa-
tion like yourself punctuates the whole of his
conversation with that awful adjective ?” “ Well,
Strson, you see, it’s this way. My father was an
pisco—blanky—palian, my mother was a Presby
—blanky—terian, and my sister turned a Roman
—blanky—Catholic, so what’s a poor to do ?”

Dear Bulletin.— To the lay Australian
mind, busy land-booming and picking doubles,
the mode of progression of the amorphous animal
known in the bulk as Christianity, seems somewhat
remarkable. The so-called Christian Church has in-
variably opposed all social reforms and decried all
scientific discoveries; it has put down its fat thumb
and said, “We know; this book is the sum of know-
ledge and the source of national greatness; any-
thing more than this is superstition, any less is
infidelity.” But whenever a new craze becomes
popular the Church plumps down on it with the
energy of a verger on a poodle, takes it into its
bosom, gives it a Christian name, accounts for its
sudden appearance by putting a new twist on
some old Hebrew text, and sends round the saucer
on the strength of it more merrily than ever.
About the last thing the Church has appropriated
is Temperance. Christianity is now about 1900
years old, and temperance has only made its
appearance during the last 100. Has the Church
been keeping its temperance up its sleeve for 1800
years? The first thing Noah did when became
out of the Ark was to get drunk and lose his
clothes, and had not the constable Kelly of that
day been accidentally drowned, some six weeks
previously, the chances are that the “ good man
and just ” would have been run in for 24 hours in
Ararat jail. Then Lot, who was especially saved
from the doomed City of Sodom, showed his
thankfulness by getting drunk as soon as he found
himself in a place of safety. (The details of Lot’s
spree are familiar to all Social Purity people.)
In fact, taking the Christian text-book right
through, it seems as if the man who kept drunk at
least eight days a week had the best chance of
heaven. Some day the Church will see money in
the totalizator, and take it unto the fold. She
will tack a tin cross upon it, give it a Christian
name, and make it holy. A little hymn and a
short sermon on such a text as “The race is not
to the swift,” with local allusions to well-known
pullings and scratehings by a parson in full profit,
would give the thing a tone. Then, when the col-
lection and maiden plate go hand in hand, and
the Church in every township embraces a “Young
Men’s Christian Teatotalizator Society,” the
millennium will be nearly due.— Borax.

THE NEW N.S.W. LAND BILL.
Extract from text:—
The period to be added to the term of a pastoral lease
in consideration of a withdrawal shall be computed so
that the added period shall bear the same ratio to the un-
expired period as the area withdrawn bears to the area
left. The method of ascertaining the added period shall
be by multiplying together the unexpired period and the
area withdrawn and dividing the product by the area left.
For the purposes of computation any fractional part of a
month shall be reckoned as one month, and any fractional
part of an acre shall be disregarded.
Rejoice, illiterate brothers,
Clap large, applausive hands,
For massive-brain’d Carruthers
Sits down to boss the “ Lands ”;
Yet from his old department
No force can tear him free :
For science still and art meant,
He’ll teach the Rule of Three.
His birch he girds with mighty words
To teach you Rule of Three.
O Schoolmaster, in earnest
Thou’rt all “ abroad ” at last;
For wider scope thou yearnest,
Lands infinitely vast —
But mark, good simple folks you.
What tender sympathee
Fills him who soft invokes you—
Then let those fractions be !
“Deal only, sirs, with integers,
And let the fractions be.”
Carruthers, bright Carruthers,
Just take the rest in tow;
Ground in three R’s those others
Who lug portfolio:
Add faith to “ Works,” re-audit
That model Treasuree—
Still, earn our wildest plaudit
For help with Rule of Three.
For grateful pause ’mid dreary laws
To dwell on Rule of Three.
Harry Furness has registered a co. to run
his comic illustrated paper, Lika Joko.
Only four short years ago the Illustrated
Sydney News said, among other fulsome eulogies,
about Matthew Davies : —“ His benevolence keeps
pace with his wealth. . . . He was chosen for
the Speakership more for his genial qualities and
strict integrity of character. . . ~ Not one of
his predecessors ever won more respect on both
sides of the House. . . , He is regarded in
society as absolutely without reproach. He is in
every way a credit to the native-born population,
&c.” Consider the uncertificated Matthew of to-
day, and how does it all read ? It is only one
more evidence that it is unsafe to praise any man
until he is dead. Also, it is better to wait until
he has been dead for a considerable time—say dead. :
The successful removal of Piles of the
most stubborn description is being effected by
Sharp’s new “Cannalin” treatment, large quan-
tities of which are being forwarded to sufferers
throughout the colonies. It is obtainable, with
advice, from 257 George-street, Sydney.
A verse from one of the Melb. Argus
election rhymes:—
See your mother there, boys.
She’s barely in her prime ;
But the gray’s on her hair, boys—
She’s old before her time.
She’s worn her life away, boys,
AH work and no play, boys—
Sixteen hours a day, boys.
For the artisan’s eight-hours’ day.
This poor drivel, which appears to be a parody, is
published “ with apologies to Mr. Sydney Dobell,”
whoever he may be. The apologies are due to
every Victorian who bought a paper in which the
rhyme cropped up.

The following death-sentence is said to
have been actually passed some years ago at Santa
Fe (U.S. A.); —
Jose Maria Martin, stand up. You have been indicted,
tried and convicted by a jury of your own countrymen of
the crime of murder, and the court is about to pass upon
you the dread sentence of the law. As a usual thing,
Jose Maria Martin, it is a painful duty of the judge of the
court of justice to pronounce upon a human being the
sentence of death ; there is something horrible about it,
and the mind naturally revolts from the performance of
such a duty. Happily, however, your case is relieved of
all such unpleasant features. The court takes a positive
delight in sentencing you to death. You are a young
man, Jose Maria Martin, apparently of good constitution
and robust health; ordinarily you might haVe looked
forward to many years of life, and the court has no
doubt you have expected to die of a green old
age ; but you are now about to be cut off in conse-
quence of your own act. Jose Maria Martin, it is
now the spring-time; in a little time the grass will be
springing up green in these beautiful valleys and on these
broad mesas, and on the mountain sides flowers will be
blooming, birds will be singing their sweet carols, nature
will be putting on her most attractive robes ; life will be
pleasant and men will want to stay ; but none of this for
you, Jose Maria Martin. The flowers will not bloom for
you, Jose Maria Martin; the birds will not sing their
sweet carols for you, Jose Maria Martin. When these
things come to gladden the senses of men you will occupy
a space 6x2 beneath the sod, and this grass and these
beautiful flowers will be growing green above your lowly
head. The sentence of the court is that you be taken
from this place to the county gaol; that you be there
safely kept and securely confined in the custody of the
sheriff until the day appointed for your execution—
be very careful, Mr. Sheriff, that he have no oppor-
tunity to escape and that you have him at the ap-
pointed place at the appointed time, until—Mr. Clerk,
what day of the month does Friday, about two weeks
from this day, come ? ‘ March 22nd, your Honor.’—Very
well, until Friday, the 22nd day of March, when you be
taken by the sheriff from your place of confinement to
some safe and convenient spot within the county, and
that you be there hanged by the neck until you are dead.
And the Court was about to add, Jose Maria Martin, may
God have mercy on your soul, but this Court will not
assume the responsibility of asking an allwise Providence
to do that which a jury of your peers has refused to do.
The Lord could not have mercy on your soul. However,
if you affect any religious belief, or if you are connected
with any religious organisation, it might be well for you
to send for a priest, or your minister, and get from him—
well, such consolation as you can, but the Court advises
you to place no reliance on anything of the kind. Mr.
Sheriff, remove the prisoner.”

a year.

city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
tv interfere.

communications will be regarded as strictly con/idential.

stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of such MSS.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 6, 1894.
The Financial Straits of Maoriland.
In last issue The Bulletin endeavoured to
sum up a few among the many varieties of
absentee who are constantly draining the
life-blood of the Australasian provinces.
The list of these foreign creditors was very
incomplete; still it was as complete as the
limits of a newspaper article would allow.
It included the Australian property-owner
who spends his rents in England ; the Eng-
lish investor who buys Australian land cheap
and sells it dear ; the English land and rail-
way syndicates, mortgage companies, invest-
ment, loan, discount and other companies ;
the English banks which do business in this
country ; the Australian banks (so-called)
which are largely English ; the English
depositors, debenture-holders, mining specu-
lators, miscellaneous financiers, importers,
insurance co.’s, the English holders of State
securities, municipal securities, harbor-board
securities, and various other kinds of
absentee creditors. Among them all,
these provinces probably owe about
£500,000,000, and have to pay annually in
interest anywhere between £20,000,000 and
£25,000,000. This amount leaves the
country every year, never, by any chance, to
return. It is not—like the interest on the
public debts of France, Britain, the U.S.,
and various other countries—paid to local
creditoi’s, who spend it locally, so that it
goes back again into circulation. On the
contrary, it vanishes utterly out of the
aggregate wealth ot the community, and, so
far as the community is concerned, the loss
is the same as if property to the value of
over £20,000,000 were destroyed by fire
annually. This tremendous drain has to be
met in gold or produce exported, and it is
one cause —probably the main cause—of all
the misery which has descended on Aus-
tralasia in recent years. Land nationalisa-
tion, Single-tax, and kindred theories only
deal with the more equitable distribution of
wealth that is in the country. The great
absentee problem has reference to the dismal
fact that the time is steadily approaching
when there will be but little wealth in the
country to distribute, either equitably or
otherwise.
* * «
The external liabilities of this country con-
sist of the interest due annually to its
multitudinous absentee creditors, and the
price of imports ef all descriptions ; and the
fund out of which this charge is to be met is
made up of the value of all exports, coin and
bullion included. When these two items, so
far as can be estimated, balance, then the
country may be assumed to be paying its
way, or thereabouts. When the imports and
the interest-charges combined—so far as
these interest-charges can be ascertained—
exceed the exports, then Australasia is cer-
tainly running further into debt, and piling
up more interest-charges to be liquidated in
the future. And the lamentable fact is that,
for more than twenty years past, the balance
has invariably been on the wrong side. The
best result yet produced in the year of
bitterest economy was that these provinces
only went a very few millions further to the
bad. So far as can be judged by outward
evidences, they are, under present condi-
tions, unable to pay interest on their external
liabilities out of income, and it is only
because the capital of the foreign absentee
keeps steadily pouring into the country—
either openly through State loans, or secretly
through all manner of private investments—
that the liability is met at all. The lean
times are those in which foreign capital
comes in slowly ; the times of fatness are
those when it comes abundantly. But it
keeps coming all the while.
• • •
So far, only Maoriland has made a serious
effort to abjure the curse of foreign capital,
and the present state of that province is,
therefore, a matter of interest. For a con-
siderable number of years Maoriland has
floated no new loans, save for conversion
purposes only ; and it has done, and is still
doing, everything in its power to prevent
the acquisition of big estates by foreign
speculators, and to discoui’age, in other ways,
the investment of foreign capital—recog-
nising that foreign capital is merely an addi-
tion to the community’s aggregate indebted-
ness to the foreign money-lender, and
another straw added to the enormous load of
interest which goes annually out of the
country. Also, it has done its best, by a
carefully-devised protective tariff, to reduce
the volume of foreign imports and to in-
crease exports, thereby increasing the aggre-
gate annual income on the one hand and
reducing the outgo upon the other. Its
policy has been successful, in a sense, far
beyond the general expectation. The private
liabilities of the province to outside
creditors, as far as can be ascertained, have
remained about stationary. Despite a large
increase of population the imports have
decreased, during the past ten years, by
about £1,000,000 per annum, while the
exports have increased by nearly £3,000,000.
The total exports are now about £2,500,000
per annum more than the total imports,
therefore the province is paying that
amount annually out of its own resources
towards the interest due to the British
creditor. Yet it is almost certain that the
result of all this effort has not in any way
reduced the aggregate indebtedness of the
country ; the most that has been arrived at
is that the debt has not increased. And the
condition of a large proportion of the com-
munity under this strain is described as one
of extreme poverty. It is almost as bad as
that of Australia, and but for the fact that
the Democratic policy which Ballance
inaugurated has shifted a large proportion
of the public burdens on to the shoulders of
the propertied classes, the state of the
Maoriland labourer would now, in all pro-
bability, be several times worse than that of
his brother on the other side of the water.
• • •
The existing condition of things is ascribed,
by the local Conservative press, to the policy
of the Democratic Government—and it is
rightly so ascribed. But, when the Con-
servative press points to it as a proof of the
utter wickedness of the Government and
the folly of its policy, and talks of a good
time coming when the Liberal party will be
overthrown, and Maoriland will boom again
through the investment of foreign capital, it
is either a knave or an ass, or a combination
of both. The Government has simply
brought the province into a condition of
solvency ; and all the existing poverty and
suffering are the inevitable concomitants of
solvency. Maoriland is now facing the day of
reckoning ; and the only way to make an
immediate burst of prosperity out of the exist-
ing materials is to postpone that day by more
public and private borrowing, and to
have a much worse reckoning later
on. Some day or other all these pro-
vinces must cease borrowing, either volun-
tarily, because they have resolved to abjure
the unclean thing, or else compulsorily
because their credit is exhausted and no one
will lend them another coin. Then they
must either live and pay interest on their
foreign liabilities out of their own resources
—or else they must go bankrupt. The diffi-
culty cannot be met by the sale of the rail-
ways, or by any similar expedient. If the
railways are sold it must be to an English
syndicate, because no company could be
formed in Australia with money enough to
buy such a property. And whether Aus-
tralasia has to send to England £8,000,000
i per annum as interest on money bor-
rowed by the State to build the railways,
or whether it has to send the same amount
to the same place as dividends on the capital
put up by English speculators to buy the
lines, the drain on the community is exactly
the same. The only alternative, providing
A ustralasia does not propose to repudiate, is
either to begin living ®n our own resources
now—which means poverty and hard work
and little hope for the future—or to face the
same problem later ©n, which means worse
poverty, and all the other evils in an accen-
tuated form.
• • •
Unfortunately for Maoriland, either the
present Government is too weak for the task
it has undertaken, or else the task itself is
an impossibility ; and a resumption of
foreign borrowing appears as one of the
features of Treasurer Ward’s latest budget.
This is a misfortune in every respect. If
Maoriland had resolutely abjured the curse
of imported capital for ten years more, it
would either have become solvent and
prosperous, or else it would have gone bank-
rupt, In either case it would have settled
decisively whether it is possible for the
Australasian provinces to meet their enor-
mous liabilities honestly or not; as no other
Government seems inclined to make the
attempt this country looked to Maoriland
for the answer to the great conundrum, and
the answer, apparently, is not to be forth-
coming at present, Mr. Ward’s borrowing
policy has this much to recommend it—that
its object is not to add to the aggregate
national burdens, but, by creating a fund
out of which money can be lent on mortgage
at a low rate of interest, to oust the private
foreign usurer; and thus reduce the total
amount of interest payable by the country
at large. But the dismal fact remains that
the good old influx of foreign capital is being
commenced afresh, and no one can foresee
the end of the experiment. If the Seddon
Government had founded its Land Bank
with a capital of, say, £5,000,000 in State
paper currency, that would also have been a
hazardous experiment, but the end of it,
even at the worst, could have been no more
disastrous than foreign borrowing must in-
evitably be. Also, it would have had the
merit of being a self-reliant policy, and
therefore more in keeping with the tradi-
tions of the Radical party. Unfortunately,
the Government lacked the courage required
for so bold an experiment.
* # •
Taking all the circumstances into con-
sideration, the probabilities are that the new
departure was not made from choice—that
it had become absolutely necessary to raise
money, in some shape or form, by internal
or external loans, and that if this had not
been done the province must have filed its
schedule. Leaving out Westralia, which is
yet in a transition state, and can hardly be
used for purposes of comparison, Maoriland
raises more money per inhabitant by
taxation than any of the other Australian
provinces, and it raises by direct taxation
over twice as much as any other, Westralia
included. Its public expenditure is less per
head than that of any other province, Tasmania
excepted ; and its expenditure for purposes
other than interest on the public debt is by
far the least in all Australasia. It has
brought economy down to the lowest imagin-
able limit, and it has piled up taxation to
an extent undreamt of elsewhere. Yet it
became necessary a little while ago to collect
the half-yearly instalment of the Land-tax
four months before the regular date of pay-
ment, in order to meet the interest on the
public debt, and this expedient seems to
suggest that the financial situation was fast
becoming more than serious. There is no
possible reason to believe that these straits
are in any way owing to misgovern ment;
despite all the failings of the Seddon
Government it is still undeniably the best in
Australasia. The whole trouble is that the
burden is too great to be borne, and when
Australia undertakes to face the position—
which she must do sooner or later—she will
go through the same, or probably a worse,
experience. It is not that the country
wants—to quote the good old daily paper
wheeze—to be developed by the aid of
foreign capital; the evil is that the country
has already been developed by foreign
capital, and that the inevitable result has
ensued.
The Latest Aspect of Theosophy.
Colonel Olcott, the blinking greybeard who
visited Australia in ’9l, was content between
blinks to proclaim himself a kind of Theosophic
John the Baptist. There was to come after
him—still between blinks—the lady Messiah
of the new Theosophic dispensation, the big
fish which H.P.B. (reverent blinks) had so
dexterously hooked. Of this high-priestess at
the shrine he, a poor servitor in the temple
(depreciatory blinks), was unworthy to kiss the
garment’s hem. (True, people had said that
Ilcott was not altogether a bug on the wheel
when there was question of rolling the mystic
old chariot along.) Then they owned William
Judge heard of William Judge yes,
he guessed (with many approving blinks) that
William was pretty spry. But Annie
Besant —“ Ah ! you will see ! ” And the
aposiopesis expired in an ecstatic paroxysm of
blinks which threatened to dislocate the
Colonel’s eyelids.
* * *
Well, wo have seen; and the good fore-
runner’s enthusiasm is intelligible. There are
no flies on Annabai. About Olcott himself
there was always hesitation. His beard left a
good impression ; but his blinks undid it. A
saint with shifty eyes is calculated to create
distrust; and the Colonel should really insist
on a better pair next incarnation. As it was
charity forbade to call him a fool, and courtesy
closed the proverbial alternative. Why will
Theosophy impale us on such dilemmas ?
• • •
Mrs. Besant has no beard (even H.P.8., with
all the adepts of the Himalayas behind her
could not grow a beard); but her eyes are
steady. And then she has made her proofs.
Theosophy hasn’t made its proofs, and isn’t
going to. Fact is, it has no proofs to make.
But that doesn’t trouble Theosophy. As
Annabai says: Here are you,\and there is
Theosophy—take it or leave it. Joe Smith
and Joanna Southcott said exactly the same
thing about their doctrines. Every religion
which starts by assuming its own validity has a
knock-down argument in the grand old for-
mula, “ Believe or bedamned !” That Theoso-
phy somewhat spiritualises the doubter’s
alternative is an evident blot on its character.
No creed should commence business without a
comfortable little hell of its town r to grill scep-
tics in. As the hell was carelessly left out of
Theosophy, it can only say, “Believe or
Karma ’ll catch you !” And that is unconvinc-
ing. Karma is so vague.
* * «
Of course, there are other blots ; but none of
them matter if you have Faith and can see
Spooks. The more Spooks you can see the
better Theosophist you are. Annabai (which
is Annie Besant’s alias in Spookland) is justly
severe on the impatient people who wish to see
Spooks as soon as they have paid their sub-
scription. It cannot be too widely known that
the subscription does not include Spooks : they
are always extra. Then you can only see
Spooks on an empty stomach. A person of full
habit, who grows stertorous and apoplectic
ever three flesh-meals daily, is an abomination
to Spooks. Whenever he approaches, they sool
Karma on to him and gather up their skirts in
flight. Why Spooks should wear skirts, seeing
that they have no legs to speak of, isn’t clear.
It is, however, a well-established Theosophic
fact that they do—perhaps because skirts hide
the medium’s hands. Spooks generally accom-
pany certificated mediums; but not always.
You can be your own medium, and see your
own Spook. But it takes a lot of evolving.
That is another blot on Theosophy : there are
no short cuts. You have to evolve a deuce of
a lot before you notice even the whisk of the
tail of a Spook flying round the corner; and,
before you can go flirting round Mountains in
your astral body with other gay Spooks after
church on Sunday evening, the chances are you
will be so wan and hollow that all the prettiest
Spookesses will cut you in disgust.
* • •
Anybody who has read this far will notice
that The Bulletin doesn’t take much stock in
Theosophy. That’s so. Theosophy is one-half
assumption and t’other half moonshine. The
name has been tagged to all sorts of queer
beliefs since it was invented at Alexandria in
the age of Greek intellectual decadence, and
the latest variety of Theosophy is about the
crankiest of the lot. It is the child of a
marriage between Buddha and Blavatsky, to
which Buddha’s common sense has not de-
scended. Buddha was a frank Materialist. He
measured Man with the Universe, and couldn’t
see why the infinitesimal parasite of such an
infinitesimal orb should preserve an immortal
identity. Neither can anybody else who has
attained a conception of the masses and dis-
tances of astronomy. As a grain of salt is lost
in the ocean, taught Buddha, so the individual
man is lost in the cosmos. As when you
separate a chariot piece from piece, taking off
the wheels and the shafts and the body, there is
no chariot left, so when Death has disintegrated
the component parts of a man there is no man
left. As a flower, in some inexplicable way,
gives forth fragrance, so man flowers into
mind ; but the fragrance dies with the flower
and the mind with the man. This is a rational,
intelligible theory, anyway. But Theosophists,
and all other emotional religionists, find it
cheerless and barren. So they create geocen-
tric myths, in which the earth, in defiance of
all probability, becomes the kernel of the
universal nut—which is just as if they had a flea
900,000 million times smaller than any flea.ever
invented, and a dog 900,000 million times larger
than any dog ever discovered, and then fixed
the flea on the extreme end of the tail of the
dog, and remarked that it was perfectly obvious
that the flea wagged the dog. Then they find
a flea 100,000 million times smaller than the
first flea, and settle him carefully on the first
flea’s back. Then they say, “ Do you see that
little fellow—flea Number Two ? That’s Us.
We boss this circus: this unparalleled dog-show
is run for our exclusive benefit, just watch us
wag !” It isn’t logical ; but it’s mighty con-
soling.
• # #
Theosophy prides itself on being consoling.
It has invented an esoteric Buddhism, which
quite knocks out the old exoteric kind.
Buddha’s Nirvana was extinction, and when
the Ego got the best of Karma and entered
Nirvana it was wiped out finally. But
Theosophy says this is all wrong : you simply
lie low in Nirvana while they are airing the
sheets of a new world, and then you begin
existence again de novo, only higher, up the
tree. Intellectual men nowadays incline to
think that one existence is enough to get tired
in ; and, with Swinburne, thank “ whatever
gods there be ”
That no life lives for ever,
That dead men rise up never,
That even the weariest river
Winds somewhere safe to sea.
But Theosophy doesn’t pride itself on being
intellectual. Its method is entirely deductive
—it starts by taking everything for granted ;
and all human knowledge which is worth
having has been gained by the inductive
method, which takes nothing for granted, but
accumulates facts and observes the law which
unites them. Thus Theosophy is retrogression.
It would bury humanity once more in the age
of Faith, and turn its back on the age of
Reason, which, in the light of science, has


everywhere succeeded the ago of Faith. And
Faith, iu three words, is belief without
evidence. # # ,
Mrs. Besant knows all this perfectly well,
gut she deems her own faith justifies her in
patting the best Theosophio foot foremost, and
keeping its crippled fellow as far as possible in
the background. Thus she exalts Theosophy
ft s an ideal religion, and disappoints those who
o to hear on what facts it is founded. And
when, with due 11 mrish and mystery, the facts
a t last appear, they prove to be the merest
trumperies. Mrs. Beeant has been in the
astral body—that is, she has felt a sub-con-
sciousness apart from her physical body. And
w hat then? , She has observed clairvoyance
and clairaudience. And what then ? She has
known writing executed at a distance from the
controlling will. And what then ? Do these
tilings prove the existence of Karma, the dogma
0 f re incarnation, the difference of the seven
principles, the truth of the complicated
theosophio machinery ? They do, in the same
sense that the pons dsinorum proves the exist-
ence of a society of highly-evolved jackasses on
the other side of the mo >n. Doubtless there
are natural forces with which we are still im-
perfectly acquainted, but they have as much
connection with the “ secret doctrine ” of
Madame Blavatsky as kisses have with comet-
tads. #
So Mrs. B esa NT, a skilled advocate, glides
over the we tk places of her own case, and
vividly displays those of opposing Materialism.
Now, there are as many holes in Materialism as
there are holes in a ladder ; but, at all events,
Materialism can show the ladder, Theosophy
can only show the holes. Materialism cannot
account for the conversion of molecular move-
ment into thought; it cannot account for self-
consciousness ; and 'it does not hope to be ever
able to account for them. But as there is not an
iota of proof that thought or consciousness ever
existed without a material basis, Materialism
furnishes the best theory available to explain
the facts. Theosophy doesn’t profess to explain
them—that is, to justify them; it simply ignores
them and substitutes the imagination of Madame
Blavatsky, whose visions appear to have come
as often and as conveniently as Mormon revela-
tions. And, though science can’t understand
how matter produces thought or consciousness,
it believes they do with as much reason as the
average person, ignorant of the theory of sound,
believes that the organ ground under his window
produces a mockery of “ Ta-ra-ra-boom-de-ay.”
In spite of appearances, the tune map be play-
ing the organ, as Theosophists assert that mind
plays man. But it isn’t likely.
* * •
Theosophy is as wrong morally as mentally.
It preaches, like other Oriental creeds, the
crucifixion of the flesh, and inculcates renuncia-
tion in this life for the sake of profit in lives
to come. It has adopted the Buddhistic doc-
trine that the way to salvation is to crush out
every desire, every passion, everything natural
and human. As if you told a gardener that the
best way to grow a tree was by taking away its
soil, keeping off the sunshine, and clipping
every twig close to the root 1 As for immor-
tality, if the worst comes to the worst, there
is no need to da penance in this world to ensure
pleasure in the next. The man who is a good
husband, a good father, a good friend, and a
good citizen on this side of the grave isn’t going
to be far wrong on the other side. Whatever
power roosts at the root of things will see after
that. In the meantime, it’s poor philosophy,
as Ingersoll puts it, “to give up skim milk
here for the sake of butter beyond the clouds.”
Some for the Glories of this World ; and some
Sigh for the Prophet’s Paradise to come ;
Ah, take the Cash, and let the Credit go.
Nor heed the rumble of a distant Drum I
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Fair, but False.
The second hearing of the Speight v. Syme
libel-case came to a charming conclusion last
week. The eleven surviving jurymen were
unanimous in their verdict. They found that
Speight had been unjustly libelled by the Age,
although the Age’s comments upon Speight
were quite fair. In effect, the jury declared
that Speight was an honest manager of the
Victorian railways not incompetent, nor
wildly extravagant, nor the creature of corrupt
politicians, nor anything that the Age alleged
in its articles—and yet a man who had laid
himself open to unmerited abuse. In the law
of. “fair comment,” as expounded by Judge
W illiams, there was no hope for the jovial
plaintiff. It gave him no chance to recover
damages from the journal which wrought his
ruin. Had the jury followed the Judge’s direc-
tion they would have found the Age charges
of reckless extravagance to be “ substantially
true.” The verdict was a flat contradiction of
Ms Honor’s very evident opinion. The jury
disagreed en masse with their oracle’s estimate
of the evidence, whilst agreeing that his
definition of fair comment, which exonerated
defendant from all legal responsibility as
regards nine out of ten libels, must con-
trol their verdict. On the tenth count, the
same that Speight had already scored a .£lOO
win with, they awarded plaintiff one farthing
damages. Judge Williams advised them not
to allow much further reparation for the one
statement which was inexcusably false, and
they fixed upon the old traditional minimum
amount. This is presumably the finish of the
great Vic. Railway Libel Case. Two juries
have dealt with it. The first dozen men were
so fogged by the involved impartiality of the
Judge’s charge that they came to no sort of
agreement, save on the single point aforesaid.
Uie second trial was presided over by a
Judge who prides himself upon never being
vague. His hostility to Speight’s side of the
case was quite clear. The public saw it stick-
ing out from the newspaper reports. Yet on
Hus occasion the eleven surviving jurymen were
unanimously favorable to Speight’s personal
and professional reputation. Sympathisers
with Speight will naturally find fault with
Judge Williams, in that he gave the Age all
credit for having said no more than it might
reasonably suppose to be true at the time
whereas he did not dwell upon the fact that the
libel time was quite different to the era of
Speight’s expensive station-building and other
acts of alleged shocking extravagance. Speight
came to Victoria in the bright dawn of the boom.
The Government during the next two years bor-
rowed 20 millions of money and gave most of it to
Speight with instructions to spend it at once.
Any number of Melbourne banks, the while, were
busily collecting British deposits, and giving it
—lending isn’t the word—to their directors and
directors’ friends. The population of the
country, according to every person who came
into close contact with the new-chum railway
ruler, was bound to double and treble itself at
a furious rate. On every hand he was told to
look forward to a glorious future, and lay his
plans accordingly. He did. He was wrong.
Would another new-chum in Speight’s place have
been less wrong, and were his boom-time mis-
calculations deserving of the punishment in-
flicted upon him —by the Age? Judge Williams
seemed to look at nothing from the Speight
point of view. Here was a circumstance that
he passed over altogether, because it wasn’t in
the evidence. Was Speight’s peremptory dis-
missal from his billet a “ fair comment ” upon
his integrity and administrative ability ? The
jury have in effect said “No” to that, and
very distinctly. But it was fair to bring a
mass of substantially untrue charges against
Speight, and achieve his overthrow by means
of those misstatements. Speight has suffered.
David Syme has suffered. We have all suffered,
and now, let us conclude with a loud “Amen.”
The absurdly melodramatic arrest of Mrs. Ven-
nell, for the alleged murder of her child at
Maryborough (Vic.), recalls to mind the Beach
(Geelong) tragedy, which remains a mystery to
this day. Some years ago farmer Beach was
found dead from a gunshot wound, his wife and
a man-servant being in the house at the time.
These more or less suspected parties showed no
disposition to leave the neighbourhood after the
coroner’s jury had returned an open verdict.
Mrs. Beach lived on in the old house, the man-
servant got married and removed to a cottage
hard by. After a while a theatrical policeman
worked up a ghost of a case and obtained a
warrant for their arrest. Both widow and ex-
servant were always available and would have
walked straight to the police-station upon
receipt of a post-card requesting them to come
along and be arrested. The pompous inspector
preferred to regard them as desperate criminals
in hiding. Late at night he went to their
respective abodes with his warrants in one hand
and a bull’s-eye lantern in the other, ordered
them to get out of their beds, and led them hand-
cuffed away to durance vile. The same course
was adopted in Mrs. Vennell’s case. She was
still occupying her cottage home, nursing the
two children who escaped death, quite ready to
give herself up to “ the authorities ” if called
for. A detective, after looking for evi-
dence at Maryborough, comes back to Mel-
bourne in a violent hurry, obtains a warrant,
returns to Maryborough by the evening train,
and takes his Guy Fawkes lantern and warrant
round to the Vennell domicile just as the
woman is getting ready for bed. The police
found her “ dressed in a loose wrapper with her
hair hanging down,” and they hurried her off
to gaol forthwith. An inspector, or sergeant,
armed with a murder-warrant against a deaf
mute who had lost both his legs, would make a
point of effecting a “ clever capture ” in the
silence of the night. The great ambition of
such people is to introduce appalling stage
effect into the simplest and most commonplace
of jobs, and a lickspittling daily press encour-
ages their nonsensical displays.
When J. B. Patterson returned thanks to
the electors of Castlemaine, after his return by
a scraggy majority, he made some pathetic
allusions to his fallen state, and remarked that
his Government had only “paid the penalty of
the odium attaching to retrenchment.” In
other words, Patterson claims that it was
because of his vigorous retrenchment policy
that he was ejected. Yet, so long as James
Brown P. retrenched and proposed new taxa-
tion to make ends meet, Victoria supported
him loyally, and gave him a steadily increasing
majority. It was only when his budget came
along, and the country realised, by reason of
the deficiency, that he wasn’t retrenching
nearly enough, and hadn’t the courage to
impose new taxation where it was wanted, that
it rose up and threw him out. And in place of
him it has sent in Turner, who is pledged to
make more retrenchments than Patterson
ever dreamt of, and to levy additional taxation
as well. Patterson’s attempt to represent
himself as a martyr in the cause of economy—
as a person who is thrown out because he
did a disagreeable duty manfully—is the
meanest thing in a very mean career. It
was his moral cowardice not the moral
cowardice of the community—which broke him
up. As a rule, in times of stress, the com-
munity, which has to pay the bill, faces the
trouble much more bravely than the Govern-
ment which has only to collect it, and if the
Government ceased trembling in abject fear of
losing its billet, and went straight ahead with
its work, it would come out much better in the
end. It is rarely that a Ministry has done so,
but when such a thing has happened the
country has generally given it an enthusiastic
support. The mistake which the Patterson
variety of politician makes is in imagining that
the community at large is as mean and timorous
and time-serving as he is himself, and shaping
his feeble and crooked course accordingly.
B. D. Morehead’s statement in Queensland
Parliament the other night, to the effect that
the Labor-party, as a body, were imported into
that country at the taxpayers’ expense, is a new
political taunt which, whether made up of truth
or the reverse, will do as much harm when
hurled backwards as when fired forwards.
When Queensland employers ruled the roost
they imported free immigrants, despite the
warnings of the politically-wise, and the hostile
attitude of the only half-employed workers, and
if what Morehead now says be true, that the old
rulers are being pushed off their perch by these
immigrants, the natural remark is— Nemesis !
Again, if it be true, then these immigrants were
imported not for love but for their labor, and
so are in the same position, as Glasses’ ob-
served, as are railway commissioners and
others. But again, the statement is not true.
Of the members who represent the Labor-party
in Parliament the history of four-fifths is
public, and to that extent rebuts the allega-
tion. Of the population of Queensland, not
more than 50,000 are free immigrants ; and of
these many have risen to commercial positions
which make them sympathisers with the More-
iiead party. It is no more true, on the basis of
natural inference, that the Labor-party out of
Parliament is constituted of free immigrants
than it is that the Labor-party in Parliament is
so constituted.
Dibbs and Copeland both denounce the home-
stead provisions in Carruthbrs’ new N.S. W.
Land Bill—which provisions are merely the
Maoriland perpetual lease idea under another
name and with slight alterations —on the
ground that they will cause a serious loss of
“revenue.” In other words, the State will
only get the rent of the land—which is really
revenue ; instead of getting the price of it in
one act, and calling it revenue when it is
nothing of the kind. Hitherto the policy of
N.S. Wales has been to sell off its assets as
fast as it could, and call the money
thus obtained “ income,” which is just
the same system as that of the spendthrift
who lives on his capital until it is all gone,
barring the fact that the average Treasurer lies
about it by implication, and says it isn’t the
capital but the interest that he is living on. If
the homestead provisions in the new law are
largely availed of, there certainly will be a
falling off in what is commonly called land
“ revenue,” and so there ought to be; if the
“ revenue ” doesn’t fall off it will only be
because the measure is a failure. All genuine
democrats are waiting anxiously for that
falling-off. The only pity is that it didn’t
come some 25 years ago or more.
The ruthless determination of organised New
South Wales squatters to “down” the unions
which form the sole link between the bush
labourers and independence, the single means
on which they can rely if they would call their
souls their own, receives every day fresh illus-
tration. With the banks behind them, the
pastoralists disdain compromise and damn
public opinion. By their deliberate breach of
the 1891 agreement without reference to the
men who joined with them to make it, by their
refusal of George Dibbs’ offers of mediation,
and again by their rejection of the similar offer
made by the Conciliation department, they have
shown that they care not what loss comes to
the country provided they attain their ends.
They insist upon ramming their own conditions,
the whole of their own conditions, and nothing
but their own conditions, down the throats of
their employes, with the result that, as
worms will persist in turning, the province
is put to great expense to preserve order.
The chief objection which the men make,
and make reasonably, is to the eighth
clause, which provides that the employer’s
representative shall be sole judge of matters in
dispute. Now, shearers are not lawyers, and
do not generally know that this condition is
powerless to deprive them of their right of
redress at law in cases of which the law takes
cognisance. By the simple’expedient of point-
ing this out in a footnote to the clause some
squatters have had their shearing amicably
carried through. But the united pastoralists
as a body apparently scorn to make even such
a slender concession. They do not wish to
treat with their workers as men to men ; they
wish to rule serfs or slaves, with heads kept
firmly under the capitalist heel. And, at the
same time, they demand protection for their
property from a Government professedly demo-
cratic. The safety of property is essential to
the stability of the State—true. But flesh and
blood, after all, have higher claims than pro-
perty. The squatters had better not go too
far.
Martha Needle, the wild, wayward Melb.
woman, who suffered from fits and glassy eyes,
and abnormal hankering after the children she
is supposed to have slain by inches, was last
week convicted of poisoning her lover’s brother.
She had threatened to kill her lover’s mother ;
apparently tried to poison another of the
J uncker family, and it is now fair to assume that
she disposed of her late husband as well as the
offspring of their union. Hence Mrs. Needle
is exceedingly likely to hang. The Law declares
her a sane murderess, inasmuch as she bought
Rough-on-rats and knew that she was adminis-
tering it for a deadly purpose. The world
knows comely Martha to be a homicidal
maniac, with selfish method in her madness,
but society votes for her execution all the same.
It were less trouble to despatch Martha than
to keep her under lock and key. She must
never again be allowed a chance to arsenicise a
fellow creature, so why not put her neck
through a hole in a noose and drop her through
a hole in one of the prison galleries ? There
are several objections to this proceeding. In
the first place, the Law, if it kills Martha
Needle, will kill her under the false pretence
that she is sane. It will make a debasing spec-
tacle of her death, in accordance with custom.
Furthermore, the Law will allow a clergyman
to turn her poor diseased thoughts “ to Jesus,”
and religion will associate a horrible tragedy
with a hideous farce. If the hanging of one
Needle had a deterrent effect upon other
Needles there would be reason in the sentence
passed upon Martha ; but, as it tends to an
opposite result, why destroy one epileptic to
encourage the rest ?
The unintentionally funny-man of the Melb.
elections was Foster Rogers, who astonished
the touts by getting in for South Yarra.
Rogers travelled solely upon his invention of a
new patent surplus-tax, which he explained at
made length to great numbers of little meetings.
The object of this amusing article is to raise
two millions sterling without the expense of
collection. On a given day—if Foster Rogers
can get his tax put into practice —every adult
male inhabitant of Victoria will receive a blue
paper, similar to a census paper, requesting him
to fill in the values of all his worldly posses-
sions, also particulars of his liabilities. After
deducting liabilities from assets, he must strike
a balance and return \ per cent, tax (in postage
stamps) to the Treasurer, and next morning
the accrued deficit will disappear like an evil
dream. Also, the wheels of commerce will
revolve one more, and the nation will feel like
a giant who has got a hard-boiled dumpling ofl
his chest. Rogers has foreseen occasional
difficulties, and provided for them. Sup-
posing that an inhabitant’s sole assets con*
sist of kitchen utensils and bedroom furniture,
to the amount of £4 195., and three fourpenny
bottles of beer —making £5 in all. Not pos*
sessing the necessary sixpence for surplus-tax
he will give the Government an 1.0. U. for that
amount, the Treasurer charging him 8 per
cent. If the “tanner” and interest be not
forthcoming before the end of the financial year
the State will, we suppose, levy upon the
defaulter’s frying-pan or water-jug, and take
out the surplus-tax in that simple way. The
large majority of the 700-odd voters who polled
for Rogers could see the absurdities of his
scheme, probably, but they made him their
member because he was earnest and well-
meaning, and told them tales of old times when
other men, in other countries, recognised their
duty to the commonw T ealth and did it cheer-
fully. The enthusiast wasn’t bidding for the
“ popular ” vote with his all-round surplus-tax,
yet he got the votes of those who could least
afford to pay any tax at all, and Harris, the
old member for Capital, was beaten on the post
by Foster Rogers. There’s a satisfactory
moral hanging to the incident.
“The Sydney College of Music, 1894.”
Among other noble attributes inherited by us
from our progenitors, the everlasting British race,
is an absorbing passion for attaching portions of
the alphabet to our names. So strong has this
passion become, that even our brightest intellects
fall into imbecility over the prospect of a C.M.G.
or a K.C.M.G. The Australian Universities
turns out B.A.’s by the dray-load. There are
among us legions of M.D.’s, M.R.C.S.’s,
F. and here and there a being so lost to
all sense of shame as to publicly affix the letters
R. to an otherwise unobjectionable name.
Soon we shall have every publican writing
M.L.Y.A., member of the Licensed Victuallers
Association ; every cabman L.C., licensed cabman ;
every policeman P.C., every rogueM.L.A., every
senile lunatic, M.L.C. “The Sydney College of
Music, ’’just launched, proposes to confer the right
upon persons passing a certain examination to
attach to their names the letters A.5.0.M., that
is, “Associate Sydney College of Music;” and
L. “Licentiate Sydney College of Music.”
The pamphlet issued by this college is nicely
printed, has a coat of arms on the outside
cover, with a lyre and a pompous Latin motto,
is a public document issued by a public in-
stitution, and may be obtained free at the
counter of Paling’s music warehouse, Sydney.
After noting that all communications are to be
addressed to the “Registrar, Sydney College of
Music,’’ one sees that the college is under the
patronage of “ His Excellency the Governor, the
Right Honorable Sir Robert W. Duff, P.C.,
G. and His Honor the Chief-Justice, Sir
Frederick Matthew Darley, Knight.” Still
further on one finds a list of vice-patrons, with
strings of letters trailing after their names like
camels at Coolgardie. Thus, in all their pro-
cessional splendor :
Yice-patroiis; The Hon. Sir John Lackey, K.C.M.G..
M. ; the Hon. Sir William M. Manning, K.C.M.G.,
LL.D., M.L.G. (Chancellor Sydney University); the Hon.
Sir Patrick Jennings, K.C.M.G., LL.D., M.L.C.; the Hon.
Sir Arthur Renwick, Kt., M.A., M.D., M.L.C. (Booh!);
the Hon. Sir Joseph P. Abbott, Kt. ; Sir William Patrick
Manning, Kt. ; the Hon. R. Burdett-Smith, C.M.G.,
M.L.C. (Yah 1); the Hon. R. H. D. White, M.L.C. (this
personage has the same number of letters fore and
aft); his Honor Judge Backhouse, M.A. (Vice-Chan-
cellor Sydney University): his Honor Judge Coffey;
Francis E. Rogers, M.A., LL.B., Q.C.; Edmund Barton,
M.A., Q.C.; P. Sydney Jones, M.D.; Sydney Burdekin,
8.A.; James R. Fairfax, William H. Paling, Critchett
Walker, C.M.G.; Henry E. Barff, M.A.; Professor Thomas
Butler, 8.A.; Professor W. H. Warren, Wh. So., M.I.C.E.
Next the reader is invited to read the names of
the Foundation Members of the Sydney College
of Music. They are as follows
Neville Barnett, F.R.C.0.; John A. Delaney, Albert
Fisher, Charles Huenerbein, Guglielmo Lardelli, F.R.C.0.;
S. Gordon L. Lavers, H. R. Maclean, A.Mus., T.G.L.;
Joseph Massey, Frederick Morley, Alexander Rea, Q. de
Oairos Rego, J. Edward Sykes, Ernest E. P. Truman,
A.R.C.0.; J. Bede White, A.Mus., T.C.L.; Montague
Younger.
The foundation members had evidently met and
elected a Council, for we find further back the
Council, consisting of fifteen gentlemen whose
names as printed are identical with the fifteen
foundation members, given above. So that the
fifteen foundation members have evidently elected
each other councillors unanimously. Then they
proceed to elect each other to offices bearing.mys-
terious names :
Warden, Hector R. Maclean, A.Mus., T.C.L.; Vice-
Warden, Montague Younger; Registrar, Joseph Bede
White, A.Mus., T.C.L.; Bursar, Charles Huenerbein;
Director of Examinations, Neville Q. Barnett, F.R.C.0.;
Chairman of the Board of Examiners, John A. Delaney.
So much for the construction of this highly-
patronised institution, which has created itself the
supreme musical tribunal in N.S. Wales, and
which takes upon itself (with no initiative from
the Education Department, the University, or
any other responsible body) to lay on hands, and
to pass on the musical apostolic succession.
In order to judge the value of this College, it is
necessary, now that we know who are in it, to find
who are left out. Looking round the Sydney
musical world, and taking conductors first, one
asks : Where is Maestro Hazon ? In pianists,
where are the brilliant Henri Kowalski and Alice
Charbonnet-Kellermann ? Where is that refined
musician Mr. Sydney Moss? Where is Mr.
Harry Hyndes ? In organists, where are Wiegand
and Augustus Gehde? In violinists, where are
Rivers Allpress, Horace Poussard and Henri
Stael? Those names are absent. So we have
in the College an ensemble with all the lead-
ing instruments left out, with no conductor, and
with the sounding brass, the tinkling cymbals
and the kettledrums trying to play lead. To
get an analogous case, it is almost necessary
to suppose that the Sydney Brush Club,
a deserving institution composed of rising
minor artists—some of them chiefly engaged in
the useful and artistic label-drawing industry-
had suddenly called itself “The Sydney College
of Art, 1894,” got together a heavy list of patrons,
and forthwith proceeded to examine candidates
and confer distinctions, titles, and diplomas, and
to re-distribute the alphabet, and meanwhile had
left outside of its ranks, and utterly ignored, such
men as Streeton, Lister, Mahouy, Ashton, Roberts,
Minns, Spence and Gollingridge. This would
have been as gross a piece of impertinence on the

Art Society of N.S.W.
With a wealth of newly-acquired wall-space,
N.S. W. Art Society makes its 15th exhibition the
best yet held, for not more than a score of the
works exhibited are unworthy of the space
occupied. It is, however, noticeable that through-
out the whole collection, with one exception,
there is not a single picture that attempts to
typify Australian life. That this exception is, in
certain important respects, the finest thing in the
gallery should be sufficient to show our artists the
field open to them. Mr. Tom Roberts—who a
year or two ago scored heavily in Melbourne with
a similar subject, for which he was paid some-
thing like 300 guineas—has undoubtedly sent the
picture of the year in his “Shearing at New-
stead ” (300), a work bearing evidence throughout
of most careful and conscientious study. This is
emphatically a picture typifying a phase of Aus-
tralian life that is rapidly passing away, and the
trustees have done wisely in securing it for the
National Gallery. Mr. Roberts’ other pictures
consist of portraits, which tend to confirm the
impression that he succeeds better when making
the best of the face of a pretty woman than when
attempting to dignify that of a prosaic man.
• • «
Mr. Lister has sent a dozen contributions,
several of them of exceptional proportions.
“ Where the Sea Tide Ebbs and Flows ” (284) is a
large picture, and in many respects the finest work
he has yet turned out. The fleecy grey clouds in
the sky and the reflections in the shallows in the
foreground are admirably treated ; but a big pic-
ture has been made out of small material. Mr.
Lister might have made an even greater success
had he painted this view on a smaller scale. The
same fault is to be found in that artist’s “ East-
ward Ho ” (240), although it is impossible not to
admire his treatment of the stately ocean swell.
“Peaceful Morn” (210), showing an expanse of
sand with a grey sky through which the sun is
struggling to force itself, is a far more successful,
though less ambitious, example of Mr. Lister’s
skill; but, taken all in all, his woodland scene,
“Nature’s Solitude” (325), with its delicate
Corot-like treatment of the foliage and the bracken
undergrowth, is the best of his contributions this
year, and is especially interesting as showing a
phase of his talent hitherto unknown.
• # #
The “Portrait ol a Lady” (239), by Mr.
Gordon Coutts, which worthily occupies a place
of honor at the end of the room, is an extremely
fine piece of solid and honest work; the features
are excellently modelled, and the treatment of the
black cloak, which really looks as if a human body
were inside it, is a masterpiece of technical skill.
Mr. A. H. Fullwood sends 12 pictures of varying
merit, but his large canvas, “Sunrisefrom Mount
Wellington, Tasmania ” (233), is the most daring
and most successful work he has ever produced,
though in places the color is overloaded. The
most successful portion of the picture is the paint-
ing of the faint purple mist in the middle distance,
and of the boulders and undergrowth in the fore-
ground. Of the artist’s other works we prefer
“A Rngoon Camp ” (209), a mob of cattle in the
water, although the drawing of the animals is
very wooden indeed. This latter fault is also to
be found with his “Newbold Ford, Clarence
River ” (333) —a subject so excellent as to be
worthy of treatment on a larger scale. Mr. Full-
wood’s figure picture, “ Bad News from Town ”
(283), cannot be regarded as a success, the man
standing over the fire being too small for his sur-
roundings, while the story the canvas is intended
to convev is not very obvious.
• * •
The exhibition shows that the “impressionist”
craze is still rampant among the younger men,
but it is pleasant to find that Mr. Arthur Streeton
(the Australian high priest of the school) has for-
saken the eccentricities of his artistic youth. He
has certainly never produced a finer work than his
“ Gloucester Buckets ” (293), with the grey trunks
of the. barkless trees standing out against the
distant purple hills and dull, leaden sky. The
tone of the picture is pitched in a low key; and
this has necessitated a restraint in the use of
which the artist has shown a new and powerful
phase of his talent as a painter. His other con-
tributions are on a smaller scale, but show the
same fine treatment of the subjects chosen, the
“Purple, Green and Gold,” a sunlight effect
(snapped up by a lady-buyer for ten guineas)
being especially successful. Mr. J. R. Ashton,
who has not exhibited for several years past, has
sent in a number of oil-paintings, also water-
colors and pastels. Of the former, “La
Malade Imaginaire” (274), a cleverly-painted
study of a child in bed, is perhaps the most inter-
esting, but it is to be regretted that the artist
did not use his great and varied abilities on more
promising subjects. His water-colors consist, for
the most part, of studies of models in different
stages of undress, to one of which the singularly
unapt title of “ 11 Penseroso ” is attached.
* * *
Mr. Piguenit contributes half-a-dozen oil-paint-
ings, all on a small scale, the largest being “ Over
the Hills and Far Away.” Conventional though
this sterling artist may be in following traditional
methods of treatment, there can be no question
that he gains the effect he aims at.
• • •
Mr. Percy Spence’s chief picture this year is
“The Long Day Closes”—two horses returning
from work in the evening, which would hare
achieved a great success were people not so
familiar with the same artist’s charming work,
“The Ploughman Homeward Plods," in N.S.W.
National Gallery.
• * •
Last year Mr. F. P. Mahony, in his “The Days
of Old,’’ made something more than a demand on
the public admiration of his technical ability, and
it is to be regretted that he has not followed the
same course on this occasion. His “Sunday” is
a study of two tired horses, resting from their
week-day labors. The picture tells no story, but
it would be hard to overpraise the drawing and
painting. The same good qualities are to be
found in his portrait of the racehorse Abercorn
(321) and “ A Veteran ’’ (250), while his portrait
of Mr. D. H. Batohen is excellent in a different
field of art. Mr. Mahony has also here a very
powerful study on a pastel of a tiger’s head,
evidently carefully sketched from life.
* * •
Mr. Sid. Long has apparently laid himself out
to startle the public, and has done so to some
extent, as the Gallery trustees have, for some
inscrutable reason, bought his “By Tranquil
Waters ” (275). Not that ample signs of talent are
lacking, but it is emphatically the talent of pro-
mise and not of performance. Here and there, as,
for example, in the limpid water of the distance
and the heavy shadows cast in the stream to the
left of the picture, some admirable work is shown,
but the effect of the whole is crude and garish.
If Mr, Long fulfills his undoubted promise, no one
will more regret than he that his early picture
should have been accorded a place in the National
collection.
# # «
There is little to note among the contributions
of the lady artists this year, save the fact that
some of the younger generation show a commend-
able inclination to break away from the trammels
of conventionality. As an instance, Miss Muskett’s
“In the Happy Morning ” (266), a girl in pink,
lying on the grass in the full sunlight. The effect
is original, and the treatment broad and forcible,
although the somewhat dingy background seems
to be out of harmony with the bright light of the
rest of the picture. Another notable contribution
to this section is “Country Visitors ” (317), in
which the key-note is cleverly given in the red
shawl of the old woman ; and praise is also due to
Miss M. Fleming for her still-life studies, which
show a strength sadly lacking in the niggling
method of most of her sister-artists.
» • •
The water colors, as a whole, do not exhibit any
special features, but if anything could redeem
them from a charge of mediocrity, it would be the
presence of Mr. B. E. Minns’studies of aborigines,
which the trustees have very properly bought for
the Gallery. Apart from the admirable technique,
the characterisation of the different types of black-
fellow is worthy of unstinted praise. The same
artist has managed to throw a fine sense of breezi-
ness into his water-color drawing “The Cows are
in the Corn ” (84), in which the figures and land-
scape are equally good. Mr. A. J. Hanson has
sent several charmingly idyllic drawings of
country-villages, and Mr. W. B. Spong has con-
tributed some Maoriland scenes and a representa-
tion of a street in Brittany; but on the whole
there is a monotony of subject which makes this
part of the exhibition uninteresting. Of the
sculpture it need only be said that, with the excep-
tion of Mr. D. H. Batchen’s “Sprinter,” there is
nothing above mediocrity, and a good deal that is
below it.
The Wild-Cat Column.
Ever since the B.N.Z. became a State-guar-
anteed institution suggestions have cropped up at in-
tervals to the effect that the Australian Governments
should guarantee, in a somewhat similar fashion, the
reconstructed banks of Australia. Such a proceeding, if
the guarantee was unconditional, would naturally com-
mend itself to many badly-scared creditors and share-
holders. It would at once raise the reconstructed
deposits to par, and the depositor, who was promised 4J
per cent, mainly because the security was rather uncer-
tain—the English creditor, as a rule, would gladly take
3i per cent, these times if he could get absolute safety
along with it—would then get his 4J per cent, and reason-
ably good security as well. Also, the State-guaranteed
banks would be able to get a lot more British deposits at a
low rate, and go in for another boom straightway. Such
an arrangement is altogether too one-sided' to commend
itself to any moderately sane Government. Still it is
possible to construct a State guarantee which would do a
lot of good to the various reconswindled institutions, and
do a good deal to restore “ confidence,’' while at the
same time it would prevent more disastrous banking
booms, and save a considerable amount of money to this
country.
The terms of such a guarantee might be some-
thing like this: First, all banks doing business in Aus-
tralia to be absolutely prohibited from taking any more
British deposits under any circumstances whatever.
Second, in the case of any reconstructed institution,
which proved, after a proper State audit, even sufficiently
solvent to pay interest on its deposits and leave a small
annual surplus, the province to which it specially belongs
to offer its creditors 3J per cent, debentures in exchange
for their present 4J per cent, deposit receipts. As Aus-
tralian 3J per cent, debentures are mostly worth within a
fraction of par, the offer would be a distinct gain to the
depositors, and ought to be readily accepted. By this
process the State would become the principal creditor of
the reconstructed institutions, and could reasonably
demand to nominate at least one-half the directorate,
and to institute a rigid system of State inspection.
Then the interest payable by the banks on their
guaranteed deposits might be reduced to 4 per
cent., instead of the present 4J. The advantages to be
gained are as follows;—(1) The interest payable to the
reconstructed British depositors would be reduced by
£200,000 per annum, which makes a serious difference in
the drain upon a country which has already to send alto-
gether too much money to England. (2) The banks would
save all round about $ per cent, annually in interest,
which, on the Immense sums involved, makes a large
saving, and in the case of some tottering institutions may
mean all the difference between solvency and bankruptcy.
(3) The State would make J per cent, per annum on the
transaction, which should build up & sinking fund suffi-
cient to cover all ultimate losses. (4) The very worst
banka would then pull through almost to a certainty,
whereas under present conditions some of them probably
will not. Even a badly-decomposed institution could get
through with a State guarantee, with a State auditor and
an independent directorate which can afford to insist
on cutting down or stopping dividends if neces-
sary, and carrying half the profits to reserve, ano
with unlimited time to realise and nurse its shaky
securities. The safety thus afforded would naturally
involve an improvement in share values. _ (5) As the State
could afford to give time so long as its interest was paid
regularly, the necessity of “putting the screw” on the
bank’s solvent debtors for realisation purposes would be
much lessened. Also, with a State guarantee and State
audit as a security against panic and fraud, the necessity
for keeping the present stupendous cash reserves lying
idle and profitless would be vastly diminished. (6) The
new departure would stop one of the many openings
through which British capital finds its way into Australia
to eat up the land with the locust-plague of interest.
Two conspicuous difficulties stand in the way.
When any portion of the money was repaid to the
depositor— i.e., the State—every precaution would have
to be taken that it was not employed for any purpose save
to pay off the public creditor. The only way to effect this
would be by special Act of Parliament, and by trusting to
the common-sense of the community not to repeal the Act.
The other difficulty is that when the main body of any
bank's creditors agreed to accept State bonds at 3J per
cent, in exchange for their deposits, a few malcontents
would probably hold out with the hope that they would
get the benefit of the State security and retain their 4&
Ser cent, as well. But these stragglers would probably
e few, and it should be quite po»sible to pay them off in
cash if they could not otherwise be disposed of.
• * • •
Says Sydney Sunday Times : “ The shares of
the Bank of New South Wales have fallen, because it is
expected that with four millions of gold locked up in its
vaults the bank cannot pay such good dividends in the
future as in the.past. Its very strength is its weakness,
so far as earning money is concerned.” As it happens,
the actual amount of gold is only about £3,000,000, but
even then, what an argument in favour of establishing a
regular local market for State securities 1 If theN.S.W.
Government would issue its 3J per cent, loans locally,
and thereby create a safe and always available liquid
security, such a bank as the N.S.W. could easily keep
£1,500,000, at least, of its present cash reserve in N.S.W.
consols. And that would mean an additional income of
£52,500 a year, and an additional dividend of about 3 per
cent, per annum. Some more loans for conversion pur-
poses will shortly be wanted in N.S.W. Any Treasurer
with a vestige of enterprise would try, when the occasion
arrives, if the local market, which rushed See’s 4 per
cent, loan when money was much dearer than it is now,
won’t respond at Si.

♦ ♦ ♦ *
According to latest advices, the shortage in the
South Melb. Building Society—Matthias Larkin’s unlucky
concern—is £100,448. Said shortage has been growing
every time it was heard of during the last two years, and
whether this is the end or only the middle is still a matter
of some doubt.
* * * •
London cablegram : “ The Freehold Trust of
Australia, Limited, has declared a div. at the rate of 10
per cent, per annum.” Hardly a week passes but some
British investment, land, mortgage or other financial co.
that The Bulletin never heard of before, announces that
it has successfully bled another 8 or 10 or 15 per cent, out
of the miserable- Australian. There seems to be no end
to these concerns, each one a sucker on one of the count-
less arms of the octopus of foreign capital.
• • * •
The “ Coolgardie Lands and Dwellings Co.” is
the latest venture before the public. Coolgardie is very
short of dwellings just now 7, and the co. proposes to build
a'great number of cheap corrugated-iron cottages—which,
in such a climate, will be another name for corrugated-
iron hells ; and reckons, apparently with some show of
reason, on letting them at 30s. a week. Even at 20s. the
co., so far as can be estimated, will realise about 22 per
cent, profit, so it can be recommended as a good, reliable,
blood-sucking investment which can hardly go wrong
unless the bottom falls out of Coolgardie.
♦ • • •
The Bulletin hears that a certain Northern
bank is really trying to live cleanly. The other day a
boom-town manager was sitting in his office when the
door opened and his successor walked in, giving him a
few hours to clear out, bag and luggage, from bank and
residence. The manager had just opened a letter an-
nouncing instant dismissal, and handing him six months’
salary in lieu of notice ; and it transpired afterwards that
the other fellow had been lying perdu in a neighboring
town for three months, studying up the accounts so that
he would not need to ask for information. Great was the
wail of the overdrafters'.

A well-known politician has profited largely by
the recent death of a certain widow. The lady, who was
about 45 years of age, taking alarm at the banks’ closing
last year, asked the legislator if he could procure her an
annuity of £2OO on her property, so that she could be
entirely free from worry. The politician, having ascer-
tained the value of her estate, agreed to accept its rever-
sion and pay her the annuity himself,
• * * •
Dear Bulletin, — No doubt it is hard that the
relatives of the school-teacher killed at Newcastle should
get nothing from the N.S.W. Superannuation Fund to
which she contributed. But relatives have no claim, and
this is where the fund has already been abused. For
instance, some contributors have none dependent on
them, others have several. Should benefits be unequal
when contributions do not differ? In England the civil-
servant has superannuation for himself (there is no fund
or contributions, for the State long ago took over the
matter on an actuarial basis), and to meet the cases of
those dependent on him there is also compulsory
insurance.—Yours, V.

The returns of Victorian gold for ’94 are the
most satisfactory of any for the group. The first nine
months of ’94 show that 543,5760 z. have been produced
for coinage. If we add to this the reasonable expectation
for the three remaining months, we have a total of
723,0000 z.— an enormous increase on the 693,0000 z. minted
for Vic. miners in '93. In fact, the increased Vic. produc-
tion is almost as important as the whole present produc-
tion of Westralia I If we take the ’9l yield (576,3990 z.)
as the standard, and reckon up the increases of ’92, ’93
and ’94 over this amount, we get a gross result of 319,030
oz. Westralia’s gross yield for the same three years is
866,4380 z. That is to say, the figures representing the
increased activity in Vic. mining are almost ns consider-
able as the figures representing the gross yield for Wes-
tralia. This, of course, eliminates Westralia’s mortgage
on the future. But Dav lesford, Steigliiz, and Tarnagulla
show that there is life in old Victoria still.

The Westralifttj gold production figures to Sept,
this year will not Vjirprise readers of this column. The
amount presented',during that time at Melb. Mint is
128,3630 z. The additional amount which may be reason-
ably expected during Oct., Nov., and Dec. is 50,0000 z.
The gross coinage at Melb. will probably be about 90 per
cent, of the gross production. This would give 196,0000 z.
as the present estimate for ’94 in the golden West.

In Switzerland 5 per cent, interest is the legal
maximum. If more is charged neither principal nor
Interest can be recovered at law ; and in oases of aggra-
vated usury the money-lender may be sent to gaol for any
term up to three years.' The statute works satisfactorily
—a sufficient answer l> t.ho-e who declare that interest
can’t be regulated otln rw i*e than by the alleged inevitable
law of demand and supply
About one-fourth of all the private railroads in
the United States —equal to 43,000 miles of line, or moi
than four times as much as all the railways of Australia
and Tasmania—are now in the hands of receivers. In
other words, they are being worked by the Slate for the
benefit of the creditors. Those who point to the U.S. as
an example of the blessings of private railway manage-
ment should make a note of this. Private management
is being superseded more and more every year by State
control, not because the State wants to get control, but
because the business is forced upon it by the constantly
accelerating collapse of the old system.

Another attempt is made to revive the Horse
Shoe Bend sluicing-mine, Shoalhaven River. It collapsed
years ago in trying to turn the river through a tunnel; it
is now proposed to wash the bed of the river by means of
a pneumatic dredge. The capital proposed seems alto-
gether too small for such a work.

BETTER TIMES AHEAD.
Some people say that life is too short, but this
opinion stands rather solitary, and most men wish
it were shorter. A certain popular Governor
wishes that his seven years engagement were at
an end next week. The lover wishes the same
regarding his engagement to Mary, whom he
would rather marry to-morrow than next year.
The clerk wishes that pay-day came round twice
a week at least. We are all looking forward to
some expected pleasure, and then live in
the hope of the next one. People are made
wretched by the lack of proper enjoyments to
divert their mind. The best is undoubtedly read-
ing, if you are alone, conversation if you choose
friends, and to crown both indulge in a Frossard’s
Cavour, a mild and aromatic smoke sold in packets
of eight for one shilling.*
