Again we are told that “The Wealth of Nations
Is as rich as ever ’’ —rather indefinite, as the public have
never been told how rioh it ever was. The fact of striking
it "as rioh as ever ” at 40ft. is said to be “all the more
gratifying to Mr. Alex. Forrest because of the attempts
made to decry the mine by his political opponents.”
Sheer nonsense. Rich mines cannot be made poor by talk,
nor can they be made rich by newspaper correspondents.
That is a matter for crushing, and if the Wealth of
Nations is “as rich as ever” it is only reasonable to ex-
pect 5000 or fiOOOozs. of gold from it almost immediately.

The epitaph of the Australian digger :—“Gold-
diggers, like the inventors of the past, benefit the world
more than themselves. The most picturesque of all figures
is that of the golddigger. The founders of Victorian
prosperity are mostly scattered about, in unknown graves,
to which they descended poor and worn-out. The digger
moves about and haunts the auriferous regions like a rest-
less phantom, until the fire burns itself out, and belies
down to die. Meanwhile, the speculator, he of the cool
head and the stony heart, he of the glossy coat and the
artificial smile, thrives and dies an honored citizen. His
body lies in a mausoleum ; the old bones of the digger lie,
God knows where 1”

“ Ma Mie Rosette,” which began well at
Sydney Lyceum on Saturday last, and promises
to go on still better, is remarkable, among other
things, as a sinful libel on the character of the
white-plumed King of Navarre. History has
hitherto regarded this monarch as a hero with a
habit of rushing to battle wherever he could find
a battle within rushing distance, and with only
one visible defect, namely his craving after
Flossies in time of peace. In “Ma Mie Rosette,”
however, he is depicted as a sovereign who incited
other men to rush to battle, that he might
lead their best girls astray in their absence.
Nellie Stewart, in a very brief red frock, is the
girl whom he wants to lead astray in this particular
instance, and the guileless Rosette is so willing to
be led, and her bucolic lover, Tapley, is so ready
to go away and batter the Spanish foe and leave
the coast clear, that it seems almost a pity the
event doesn’t come off. Bub Rosette has such
a horrid dream in Act 11. that she abjures the
King, and Tapley stays at home after all. There-
fore the lovers are married, and probably in after
years, when the wind whistles through their cot-
tage, and the glamor has worn off their love, and
Tapley is an elderly plain agriculturist dozing by
the chimney-corner and uttering loud snorts in his
sleep, she feels very, very sorry that she ever had
that dream, and wishes she had gone off with the
joyous King of Navarre, and been a sinful
Duchess.
# # #
Rosette, as already mentioned, is played
by Miss Nellie Stewart in a very brief skirt and
an air of rustic simplicity. The general verdict
of the crowd which went out to have a drink
between the acts was that she is the best thing
Our Nellie has yet accomplished, and The Bulle-
tin will let it stand at that. Brownlow started
his part as the King with ten separate colds
which brought his lower notes into great
tribulation, especially when he warbled the
touching appeal beginning “ Ma Mie Rosette,”
and seemed to be weeping in his long
brown boots. Bub for all that he looks a stately
and beautiful king, and why any girl should
refuse to go away with him at once is a
mystery. Lauri plays his comic part with tremen-
dous vigor, being assisted in his fell purpose by
the bright and capable Miss Marietta Nash, and
the dancing of this pair is a marvel to witness.
The other notable characters are a battered and
war-worn colonel, by Howard Vernon; Tapley as
a melodious lover with a comparatively small part;
and Miss Flor ice Young, who is supposed to
have fallen before the tale commenced, and who
leads a gorgeous but wicked existence in the
chateau. Miss Young seems to have good times
in the chateau, and if she is inserted as a proof of
the advantages of virtue she is an utter failure.
* * * 9ff
“ The Shamrock and the Rose,” re which
this paper made some kind observations in the
past, is again doing well at Her Majesty’s. As a
moral lesson it is. unexceptionable, for the wicked
man hardly gets a show all through, and he never
starts a scheme which doesn’t instantly fall in
pieces on his head. Everybody who thinks of
being a villain ought to have a look at this drama,
and when he sees how the bad character is insulted
and dragged around by the hair through four acts,
he will reform right there. If he doesn’t, his case
is incurable.

The Thorn ton-Arnold season at Sydney
Criterion closed with two nights of “Captain
Fritz,” and Mr. Arnold has now moved on to trill
his plaintive German melodies to other ears. A
number of enraged bookmakers would gladly go
after him_ and end his melody with a saw if the
opportunity only offered. Mr. Arnold is the most
consistently lucky backer of horses in the pro-
fession, and while Thornton occupied the stage he
had nothing to do save go out with his heavenly
smile to various racecourses and back winners.
** * *
“ The Octoroon,” by Miss Maggie Moore
and co,, was announced for last Wednesday, at
Sydney Royal, when Salem Scudder, presumably,
took the opportunity to once more advance his
theory that the camera can’t lie. Which theory
is only half true, for if the camera doesn’t lie the
photo, tells the truth with extreme difficulty.
Some further remarks on this subject are post-
poned until next week.
** * *
Mrs. Besant’s lecture on “ Mahatmas ”
was wisely reserved till about the close of her
Sydney season. The matter which the community
most wanted to hear about was the mystic
Mahatma, and if it had known earlier what a
hopeless disappointment he really is it would have
been bad for business. The artful Smythe pro-
bably realised this fact and acted accordingly. To
become a Mahatma, it seems, one must first study
for 12, 24, or 36 years. The nature of the studies
isn’t explained, but apparently they consist
mostly of vague, unspecified meditation after the
fashion of those philosophers who became wise by
fixedly contemplating their own stomachs. Then
the student must marry and rear children, and
when they are old enough to shift for themselves
he goes out into a forest and studies for the rest
of his life. After that he dies, and is reincar-
nated, and studies some more, and keeps
on dying and being reincarnated and study-
ing until he attains a state of perfection.
Having reached this state, he lives to teach
others, though what he teaches is also left
unexplained. The Mahatma idea seems to be a
development of the old-time notion that the way
to attain wisdom was to lead a lonesome existence
in a tub, and discharge sour and abusive epigrams
at passers-by.
« « «
Mrs. Besant claims that the existence of
Mahatmas is absolutely proved because the late
Blavatsky has seen them, and Colonel Olcott has
seen them, and she has seen them herself. The
general opinion is that the lamented Blavatsky
was capable of saying that she had seen anything,
and that Colonel Olcott was capable of seeing any-
thing. As for Mrs. Besant herself, she has doubt-
less seen somebody who said he was a Mahatma.
Up to date, it would appear that Mrs. Besant
doesn’t believe in the old material devil, yet he has
also been seen by thousands of apparently honest,
though rather idiotic, witnesses. So has the
sea-serpent, and the Flying Dutchman, and the
weeping ghost of Pontius Pilate, and the Wander-
ing Jew. Altogether the lecture on “Mahat-
mas ” was a disappointment, and the news that
the way to become a Mahatma is to go out and
meditate for years in a damp forest, and become a
long-haired and taloned wild man of the woods, is
so depressing that no one save a confirmed loafer
is likely ever to take to the business. Moreover,
the man who leads a nomadic existence in the
woods generally becomes a naked lunatic instead
of a seer, and is captured at last by the police
because his feet get entangled in his beard when
he tries to run away.
** * *
Messrs. Abell and Klaer’s circus, which
has taken up its location in Redfern, Sydney, is a
very middling circus in all the ordinary essentials
of the business, but in the matter of monkeys and
dogs it is certainly pre-eminent. Our unfinished
forefathers are the main features of the pro-
gramme, and their unwonted brilliance lends an
air of novelty to the business, for which this paper
is duly grateful.
** * *
“ Sowing the Wind,” although as improper
as “Mrs. Tanqueray ” in some respects, was the
most popular success of the B. and B. season at
Melb. Princess’s. By dating his play a good 60
years back Sydney Grundy took the edge off its
pungency. Time seems to have mellowed his
fruity incidents, and, like old port, they warm the
heart without burning the throat. Naughty Helen
Gray, the heroine’s mother, died six weeks before
the curtain rose, anyway. The characters who so
frankly discuss her buried shame must, in the
ordinary course of nature, have been buried them-
selves ere this. Consequently, the sassiety that
objected to the modernness of Paula Tanqueray’s
frailty is not necessarily shocked at Mr. Grundy’s
piece. Even a dress-circle may pardon the errors
of long age, and if a strong dose of declamation
be offered to the “young person ” retrospectively,
so to speak, she is entitled to swallow it and
smack her ruby lips afterwards.
* * *
The 8.8. performance in “ Sowing the
Wind” did not advance much nearer to perfection
as the week went on. Mrs. Brough and
Titheradge, asserting the wrongs and rights of sex
and sex, made far too much noise. They raised
their voices one against t’other, as though they
were arguing the point before a deaf referee, and
expected him to decide in favor of the sex that
shouted the louder. Mrs, Brough won. When
she fired off the name of Helen Grey poor
Titheradge could raise no more thunder. He
replied with a feeble gurgle in the throat, and was
carried off to bed. Melbourne looks forward to
more “Sowing the Wind” at some future time.
This, the closing week of the season, was set
apart for short revivals, and “ The Amazons ” is
the bill for the last two nights. Herein Mrs,
Brough makes her farewell appearances in Mel-
bourne before paying a complimentary visit to thei
land of her birth.
* * *
“ Morocco Bound,” from Sydney, will be
set up at the Princess’ on Saturday, with old
friend Elton and Wilfred Shine to the fore, as.
before.
** * *
A sad and thoughtful contemplation of
“The Bush King,’’ at Melb. Royal, moves the
present writer to mistrust certain statements as to.
its London success. London can stand a good
many things in the melodrama line, but the cock-
ney gods have been known to heave turnips at
entertainments not inferior to this. There is
plenty of plot in “ The Bush King.’’ The rush of
senseless murders, unexpected appearances, and
absurdity in general is well sustained, but as
much may be said of a Punch and Judy show.
The young Australian author in this case has no.
apparent idea of constructing a powerful situation,,
whilst his dialogue is balderdash without an occa-
sional pinch of ginger to fortify it. Moreover, he
is strictly humourless, and his most original senti-
ment deals with young Australian anxiety to.
“unfurl the British flag” in the face of Britain’s
foes. “The Bush -King ” will not be wanted for
more than a week—in fact, such libels upon local
literary ability are not wanted at all. On Satur-
day the same co. will apply themselves to “The
Irishman,” another new drama.
The Alhambra variety show ended a long
struggle with adversity on Saturday last, and the
iron gates of Frank Clarke’s temple are closed for
the present. Meantime Coghill Bros, are rolling
in silver at the Oxford, nee Gaiety; and the Bijou
Theatre upstairs has definitely arranged to open
on the 13th.
»% & *
A slight smattering of audience was
apparent at Melb. Alexandra when Inigo Tyrrell
introduced a Russian tragedy in seven distinct
bits. The curtain is down, generally speaking,
during the course of Inigo’s latest, but each time
that the tragedy bursts into view it betrays
lavish tendencies in the matter of scenery and
soldiers. “The Czar’s Decree” is an imposing,
though fitful, production at the price. Augustus
Glover has been specially engaged to persecute
the Jews in St. Petersburg, and the cries
of the Chosen People as they flee from
a Christian pork - butcher who wants to make
them devour pig’s feet, are something terrible.
The pork butcher forms only a passing incident,
however. Glover is the real villain of the piece.
He murders a Hebrew banker and bowstrings the
widow of his victim, after which he charges their
orphan daughter, a sweet girl in top boots, with
the crime. Glover is finally overtaken by the
Czar’s Hypnotic Deputy, who forces him to
confess all in a mesmeric trance. When several
faint visions have appeared in the trunk of a tree,
the trunk leans over towards the criminal’s head,
and a missing document meets his eye. This
settles Augustus, so the persecuted Jews return to
their homes in peace. Business at the Alexandra
will not result in many more spectacular dramas,
unless the management intends to hang on until
the auditorium is absolutely empty at 8 p.m.

Cyril Tyler’s voice seems to have gone in
the middle—presuming that it ever had a middle —
but his high notes are still strong and clear. Also,
the higher his note the less womanly its tone.

At Poverty Point.
Stage dogs are unlucky. Only the other day this paper
regretfully obituarised the bull-slut in “Morocco Bound”
when that affectionate animal was mangled by a Sydney
tram. And now Stacey’s big St. Bernard, which has been
playing “Hans the Boatman” with great success in
Adelaide, has got underneath another tram, and gone
the way of all theatrical dogs.

that establishment refused to allow the children to go, as
he had seen the play (“ Struck Oil ”) and “did not con-
sider it fit tor children to witness I ”
H. J. Samuell, publisher of Herr Juncker’s song, “I
was Dreaming,” so successfully sung by Miss Nellie
Stewart in “Ma Mie Rosette,” has consented to pay Miss
Stewart a royalty of 3d. per copy on all copies sold by
him in Australia from Saturday last. Mr. Samuell
reckons that this will mean, at least, £5OO for the prima
donna’s share—not bad for a song which she sang without
consulting either publisher or composer.
The fact that Bentley was the first man to reduce the
wages of supers, scene-shifters, and door-keepers below
the recognised tariff will be remembered in Maoriland
long after the fact that he played “Richelieu” with a
powerful Scotch accent has been forgotten. The evil that
men do lives after them, but the interesting circumstance
that their French cardinal was obviously born at Falkirk
is oft interred with their bones.

A correspondent writes The Bulletin that “ A well-
known young lady is in a terrible state because her idol,
a good-looking juvenile actor who had faithfully pro-
mised her all sorts of things, after he had succeeded in
gaining her young affections, &0., has married some one
else.” The statement that he succeeded in gaining her
young affections, &c., is a remarkably neat way of getting
over a remarkably difficult situation.
Mme. Christian retires finally from public life with the
farewell concert given to her in Melb. Town Hall,
She has been singing in Australia for 23 years—
her first appearance being at W. S. Lyster’s benefit con,
cert; after that she sang in conjunction with every noted
artist —Jenny Claus, Di Murska, Arabella Goddard,
Santley and Cowen ; with the last in 28 of the 30 oratorios
given by him during the Centennial Exhibition. She sang
with splendid voice in Mors et Vita a few months ago.
She retires because, as she succinctly says, “an artist
cannot live upon air.” The present style of entrepreneur
will not pay artists’ fees while he can get unproved singers
to spoil their voices at the rate of a few shillings a night.
For four years Mme. Christian has been in bad health.
Curiously enough, her first public appearance was, and
her last appearance will be, in a church. She was singing, as
a girl, in the Dockyard Church at Woolwich (Eng.), where
the organist was her music-master. The leading soprano
was suddenly taken ill, and she timidly volunteered to
take the part. W. S. Lyster was in the church, and
having heard her sing “Hear my Prayer,” made her an
offer to come to Australia. Her last appearance will bs
at St. Mary’s, Sydney.

Haoriland.

cally boycotted. Brough and Boucicault passed Auckland
by, as terms could not be arranged for Abbott’s Opera

gave Wellington the “ cut out.”

correspondent) begins his London campaign at Terry’s
Theatre, August 30, with “ The Foundlings.” His venture
is a risky one, for the play-going public have never quite
taken to Terry’s, and there is much in fashion.
Marie Elster and Josephs, the basso, have engaged to
join the coming variety company at Melbourne Bijou,
and the conjunction of these first-class concert names is
bound to draw people who don’t usually—haw!—patronise
“two, one, and sixpenny” shows. Miss Leslie Norman is
another strong card. John Gourlay comes forth as chief
comedian; also, a ballet of eight is in training for this
event.
A lady with a singularly thin voice gave the gallery
boys an opening on the first night of a recent Vie. per-
formance. The gods angcored her in the middle of her
verses r.nd shrieked in unison with her top notes, the
result bung a special advt. in next day’s papers that she
would take a night off. Whereupon the lady sent a
lawyer’s letter demanding £250 damages or an abject
apology, and the management put the indignant epistle
in a frame. The question as to whether a man who
engages an inferior vocalist to sing is justified in jeering
at her failure may be discussed in Court. In which case
the lady will probably produce a copy of an evening
paper containing a gorgeous notice of her “guyed ” per-
formance. The jammy “criticism ” was contemporaneous
with the managerial insult.

Mendelssohn, the Melb. photoer, shows a mass of the
amateur skirt-dancers, who have been publicly posing.
In the pictures they are smirking and holding up their
legs. Thus do a lot of foolish girls endeavor to advance
themselves in the marriage-market. The brainy party
goes to one extreme, and the leggy to another. Parents
would be agonised at the idea of any of these poll-parrots
becoming a Lethbridge or Grey. Even Mrs. Talfqwfat
entreats the principal of Melb. Presbyterian Ladies’ Col-
lege not to turn her girls into Melbas.

When Patterson and Carter, with a big
majority at their backs in Yic. Assembly a
majority which was growing with almost every
bye-election—produced their famous “tariff re-
form ” Budget, S.M. Herald announced
that the great anti-Protectionist reaction had
begun. And when the House rejected the Budget,
and the Patterson party was utterly smashed at
the polls, and three Ministers were rejected, and
Patterson himself got in by the narrowest possible
shave, and the Labor party —which went solid for
high tariff—returned almost every one of its
candidates by a big majority, then the same
Herald put the announcement, in big letters, on
its placards : “ Great Victory for Tariff Reform.”
Eight Hour Day in Sydney was, in every
respect, a successful holiday—as, indeed, it
deserved to be, seeing with what earnestness the
problem of enjoyment was tackled. The Asso-
ciated Trades walked in procession through the
streets, making a gallant show ; and then went to
Rosehill racecourse for a day’s sports. Picnickers
swarmed the harbor resorts, and judging by the
dishevelled appearance of the stragglers who
filled the streets till nearly midnight the day
was celebrated most vigorously. One poor
woman with her hair down, and a baby on each
arm, paused opposite the post-office about 10 p.m.
to mop her forehead and call to her husband, who
was bringing up the baskets and the rest of the
family in the rear, “Well, George, my legs is
dead to the world, and I’m as wet with
sweat as a dish-clout, and the children’s
as tired as tired can be; but we’ve had
a real good time, haven’t we?” And the
wretched George, towing a procession of young
Australians which seemed to reach right down to
Circular Quay, admitted that it was so. Good,
valiant George ! Long may he live to share in
the pleasure brought by the holiday which shows
the Australian worker at his best, and commem-
orates the best triumph that his enterprise has
won.

Personal Items.
N.S.W. Agent-General Saul Samuel was
74 on Tuesday, Oct. 2.
A Bourke (N.S.W.) paper has changed
editors seven times within six months.
A great statesman came to terms with his
recalcitrant usurer the other day. Terms of settle-
ment undisclosed.
Mannington Caffyn is going to publish in
book-form (through Methuen, of London) his
Bulletin short stories.
Speaker “ Joe” Abbott has resigned three
times as a member of the Athenaeum Club, Sydney.
He is off the roll just now.
B. R. Wise has not written any novel.
Any fiction he may have to spare from his law-
practice he uses in politics.
Speight, the day after his farthing verdict,
turned up in Oollins-street with a particularly
aggressive flower in his button-hole.
A Supreme Court Judge has evidently
made up his mind to secure a minor judgeship for
his son-in-law at the earliest possible date.
Baronet O’Loghlen’s father was the first
R.C. Queen’s Counsel. Bryan is by descent
Prince of Burren in County Clare, begorra.
Mrs. Needle, the convicted Melbourne
poisoner, had, at her trial, the apparent sympathy
of all the witnesses who knew her intimately.
Francis Dillon Bell, late Agent-General
for Maoriland, will be 72 on Monday (Oct. 8),
and George Dibbs will be 60 on Friday (Oct. 12).
“ Begorra,” said the late Martin Loughlin
on the occasion of his return from Europe, “ I
stayed so long in Paris that I almost became a
Parasite.”
An M.L.A., a late denouncer of Sabbath-
breaking in a debate in N.S.W, Parliament, en-
gaged in negotiations during last week for a share
in a Sunday newspaper.
Mrs. Besant, when at home, “ prefers an
Indian grain called dahl to any other”—the
identical pottage of lentils with which Jacob
bought Esau’s birthright.
Premier “ Dick ” Seddon, who also han-
kers after a K.C.M.G.-ship, laughed heartily over
the Mayor of Auckland’s cable for the York
couple to give the northern city a call.
Ex-Premier Dibbs’ unsuccessful Tamworth
rival, Turkey Piddington, jun., has not been for-
gotten by his party in the hour of need. He is
now Crown-prosecuting on Wagga Circuit.

at Toorak (Vic.) election, is descended from

Cambridge, he sent Daisy Hopkins to gaol without
trial.

THE “LONE HAND” MINE.
“On 22nd Aug. the Day Dawn South public Battery cleaned up a crushing of 41 tons
from a claim just on the boundary of the Day Dawn township, called the ‘Lone Hand’
The result was 6oz. 6dwts. per ton. Said claim, which was hardly known, is worked by
a man named Thomas Parks, with his wife at the windlass. They will nett about £1000
for their 18 months’ work.”— BULLETIN. corre[?]ondent.
MRS PARKS: “ Have you made up your mind about Woman Suffrage yet,
Thomas ? I also want a new bonnet.”
THOMAS: “I regret, my dear Sarah, the times are nob yet ripe—at least,
give the handle another turn or two and we’ll discuss ”
HIS PARD: “Decide now—or down you go!”
The man’s hat fashionable in Melbourne
just now is called the “Rothschild ’’—perhaps in
graceful allusion to the condition of the com-
munity’s finances. Thirty years ago a German
named Muller killed an old gentleman named
Briggs in an English railway-carriage, and was
hanged. He was traced and identified by this
very kind of hat, which thereafter became odious
and was known as the “ Muller cut down.” It is
a sort of bell-topper in reduced circumstances.
It must not be forgotten that the medical
gentleman who is now doing so much to boom
Spiritualism in Sydney, wrote several columns in
The Bulletin to show that the Marble Man of
Cow Flat was, or had been, flesh and blood.
An old Sydney man asks us (as if we could
tell him) how it is that so many of the Agents-
General in London take to whisky-drinking. At
least two of them—and until recently three—are
usually to be found in the afternoon in a most un-
businesslike condition.

The day after his rejection for Morningfcon,
£ £ Smith drove down Oollins-street in an open
carriage-and-pair, with two liveried servants, his
wife and four infants in the trap, and a boy and
a girl riding on horseback alongside. It pays to

The Parkes who took the chair for Annie
Besant is the identical gentleman who refused to
allow Richard Anthony Proctor, the astronomer,
to deliver scientific lectures in Sydney on Sunday
nights. Had Mrs. Besant arrived a few years
back, Parkes would have had her roasted. The
old man is progressing.
Young D. L. Dow, who only lost the seat
of Kara-Kara (Vic.) by 20 votes, is a son of the
well-known Radical ex-M.L.A. and pressman,
“Jacky” Dow. D.L. is a mere youth of rather
distinguished appearance, and besides being a
politician born, is one of the most promising of
the younger Yio. painters.
Mr. Cruikshank, M.L.A. (N.S.W,), is to
marry Miss Ethel Clarke, second daughter of
Baronet Clarke, of Victoria. Mr. Cruikshank is
accounted the richest man in N.S.W. Assembly,
and owns about half the constituency he repre-
sents Inverell. He never speaks in the Assembly,
but he has evidently been talking to Miss Clarke.
Millionaire Angas, of S.A., is 71 on
Friday, Oct. 5. His father, who lived until past
90, arrogated to himself the title of “Father and
Founder of 5.A.,” and the family paid Edwin
Hodder to write a biography of the old man and
a history of the province, with a view of per-
petuating the claim. These books have been
circulated free among the country institutes of
S.A., but up to now old man Angas is not looked
upon as a George Washington.
For years Dr. Madden (now Vic. [Chief
Justice) regularly attended the and
floggings at Melb. Gaol. Once he took with him,
as a treat like, his father-in-law, Frank Stephen,
the attorney, to see a flagellation, but the per-
formance so sickened the poor old gentleman that
two reporters had to carry him out and dose him
with brandy. The other day C. J. Madden passed
his first death-sentence —on Jordan, the negro—
and last week ordered his first flogging, the tor-
tured one being a hapless youth of 18.
When N.S.W. Justice Foster retires,
which must be soon, Attorney-General Simpson
will have an outside chance of a seat on the
N.S.W. Supreme Court Bench. The Bulletin
says “outside chance,” because Mr. Simpson
must now be well on towards 60, and as it is quite
unlikely that he could face 15 years of Bench-
work (the period prescribed for voluntary retire-
ment on pension), it is not very probable that he
would take the post, for which he is otherwise
eminently fitted. Neither Salomons, Q.C., nor
Pilcher, Q.C., would take a judgeship, and though
C. B. Stephen would be a most eligible man, yet the
public don’t want any more Stephens on the Bench.
Probably the Govt.’s choice will ultimately rest
between Toby Barton and Frank Rogers. As to
the Attorney-Generalship, in the event of Mr.
Simpson going on the Bench, B. R. Wise, no
doubt, would have the offer, but he probably
would not accept, and, there being no other com-
petent Freetrader available, Premier Reid would
have to look to H. E. Cohen, who held a law-
office years ago, and who would no doubt consent
to go to the Upper House.
Ebenezer Ward, in a recent S.A. Legisla-
tive Council discussion, referred to farmer Russel]
as “the penultimate speaker.” Whereupon
Russell arose indignantly on a point of order, to
demand the withdrawal of the epithet. Squelched.

babies and domestic servants.” Grainger, alas !
has had an experience of women enough to sour
anyone.

in his 61st year, leaving
behind him 250,000 sovs.
of which 5000 go to
Mother Church as a

time the present writer
saw Martin Loughlin the

mouth of a shaft cutting

time, Martin was sitting

clusive services of old
Dan, once of Scott’s, the
best waiter who ever
carried a plate in Austra-
lasia. It was indeed a
change from the old BalJ
larat days to a cycle of
luxury and the daily
visits of the barber—for
Martin had his hair
curled regularly once
every 24 hours. Martin
Loughlin was a bachelor,
and a very close-fisted
bachelor, too. His life
presents no particular
moral—except, perhaps,
that in all turf and min-
ing transactions it pays
best to keep your mouth
shut.
lb was to the lately-deceased Dr. Black, of
Melb., that Salmon Wilson and his family owed
their first rise. When a N.S.W, squatter, he gave
Wilson and his brothers a letter to the Strachans
of the Western district, from whom the present
millionaires got work droving sheep to the Wim-
mera—a job so well performed that they received
a bonus in sheep, which formed the nucleus of
their fortunes.
Melbournian: “They run in all shapes.
Mentally I kodaked Premier Turner rushing for
his train the day he got the Premiership. Nobody
looked at him, nobody knew him —a commonplace,
smallish, fatty man, in a brown tweed suit, baggy
trousers, very short sac coat, and very low-
crowned pot hat. He had a parcel under his arm,
possibly a new pair of boots. How different from
the conventional type of prancing Berry, or
slinking Patterson, always in the chimney pot
and frock-coat! Patterson, though, is quite
radiant, not at all with a hang-dog look, and
makes a point of being on hand on the Block.”
That wealthy old oddity, Dr. Black, of
Melbourne, has left a will which much disturbs
the minds of his numerous descendants. He has
decreed that not one of them, save the daughter
with whom he lived, shall get a cent until his
youngest grandchild comes of age—some 13 years
hence—which is rough upon all those who came
of age years ago. Dr. Black was stone-deaf and
almost blind when he died. He had a special
aversion for Sleight, undertaker of St. Kilda, at
whom he used to shake his stick in the street and
call out, “You’re waiting for me, I know, but you
haven’t got mo yet.” But Sleight did get him at
last.

he would not have got the Speakership.
Rumored that W.A. Premier Forrest will
shortly go to London as Agent-General.
N.S. Wales revenue from Ist January to
21st September was .£91,508 less than for a similar

The new Vic. Parliament will contain 17
Roman Catholics. The fact denotes the decadence
of religious bigotry in Victoria.
Wise goes to England shortly—in a Privy
Council case. He will thus be conveniently absent
when old man Parkes starts to screw Reid.
The demand for police protection in
N.S.W. backblocks has caused over 200 constables
to be drawn from Sydney raetrop. district.
Five young Australian natives in the new
Vic. Ministry, also five lawyers ; eke two mossy
Conservatives. Good, bad and indifferent.
Will the youthful Best, Vic.’s new Minister
of Customs and Lands, persist in his elective
Ministries notion now that he is safe in the arms
of two fat portfolios ?
Premier Kingston (S.A.) is at least sincere
in his absentee-taxation proposals. He is attorney
for an English estate which draws thousands a
year in Adelaide rents.
• Old man Parkes feels the desertion of W.
U. Sutter as keenly as he did that of F. B. Sutter
some years ago. Parkes then said that all
deserters “deserved to be shot.”
Professedly democratic Maoriland Min-
isters Seddon, Ward, Reeves, M’Kenzie, and
Cadman have accepted the recommendation that
they take the title “ honorable ” for life.
The “South Australian Company,” which
draws tens of thousands of pounds every year out
of the province, gets all its printing, down to the
commonest memorandum-forms, done in London.
The Australian Star prefers a Governor
to whom his official salary is of small account.
But wouldn’t a phonograph and a rubber-stamp
be better 2 And these wouldn’t want a salary
at all.
Premier Kingston states that the chief
desire of his Govt, is to bring about Federation on
democratic lines for clearly defined national pur-
poses, at the same time safeguarding local interests
and provincial independence.
Cabled that Chinese commerce flies to
neutral flags. Just as that of the U.S. did in the
Civil War, and as that of England will in war.
Look at the thirteen miles, in and out, of shipping
at Liverpool, and ask what navy could protect the
English mercantile marine.

Itwas the Australian-Irish vote that put
out Bryan O’Loghlen at Port Fairy. The
Australian-born R.C. youth, irrespective of
nationality of parentage, can’t be depended upon
t® cast his vote as Father O’Gilligan directs with

G. H. Reid, as a barrister, defended
banker Abigail, and had a tremendous flare-up
with Judge Windeyer, re some points which he
claimed had been reserved, but upon which the
Court, at the instance of Judge Windeyer, refused

and holds Abigail’s case in the hollow of his hand.
When a young Radical with a very rich
Tory is given by that father a choice
between voting Tory and being absolutely dis-
inherited ; when, moreover, he is told that if he
again stands in the Democratic interest he will be
opposed at the poll, tooth and nail, by the old man
himself—well, no wonder that young man stands
down.
The Argus published a list of 42 candi-
dates for Yic. Assembly “ recommended ” by the
Freetrade party—most of them being Protec-
tionists all the same. Twenty-six out of the forty-
two were rejected, and among the throw-out were
seven members of the late House. So much for
the Argus’ discovery of a general outcry in favor
of a reduced tariff.
From the Question and Answer column of
Melb. Herald : —“Wager wishes to know, through
the medium of your valuable paper, who is heir to
the throne when the Queen dies.” Here is an
instance, apparently, of two or more loyal Aus-
tralians entertaining a strong feeling of attach-
ment to the Royal Family, but not quite clear
as to why the Western Plains echo “ God Bless
the Prince of Wales !”
Speaker Bent died fighting, at the bottom
of the poll, like a man. Bent is a man—not a
desirable man for Vic. Parliament —but a man,
nevertheless. He made as dignified a little speech
as he knew how to make when the groaning mob
allowed him to acknowledge his licking, and it
may_ be fairly said of T. Bent that nothing in his
public life befitted him better than his way of
leaving it.
There does not seem to have been the
faintest suggestion of venality about Speight’s
management of the railways. No line deviated
from the route originally planned; nothing was
done in the dark. Speight might have built less
substantial stations and less durable lines, but,
seeing that he was ordered to spend so much
money in a certain time, it is clear that the pre-
sent railway deficit must have existed under any
circumstances.
The great joke of the Yic. elections is
the plaintive cry of Brewer M'Cracken that he was
wantonly deceived by about 500 Deakinites, who
promised to give him their warm support. These
dissimulating ruffians cheered M'Cracken accord-
ing to promise, but gave their votes to Deakin.
Then, having voted for the other man, they came
forth from the polling-booths and cheered Mao.
more warmly than ever,

so much as the representative of a certain small
crowd (which he always was and ever will be) as a
member for Victoria at large. No purely little
local ticket will have any weight or influence with
him, he says. This, in view of the hordes of
Castlemainiacs who infest the public departments,
is a staggering statement.

S.A. Govt, did a smart thing over the
Beer Duty Act. The measure was assented to in
Ex. Council half an hour after it was passed, and
Customs officers were in the breweries an hour
later! Apropos. When the increased Probate
Duties Act was passed last year, Kingston got
word the same evening that a prominent million-
aire was dying. An Executive Council was held
at Adelaide Govt. House at 1 a.m. That million-
aire is still alive.
Some “free” shearers despatched out
West (N.S.W.) were sworn in as special con-
stables, and ten of the crowd were armed with
rifles at State expense. In the strikes of recent
years, when unionists offered themselves as special
constables, their proffered services were jeeringly
refused, on the excuse that they were parties per-
sonally interested in the current industrial dis-
putes. But the meek and gentle blackleg is a pet
creature of quite another sort, and worthy of all
cheap honors.
Dear Bulletin, Please notice that
shearer delegate Toomey’s (Young, N.S.W.)
manifesto to the union men appears in the Melb.
and Sydney press under such headings as : “A
violent manifesto,” “An extraordinary mani-
festo,” “An insurrectionary document,” “A
daring manifesto.” The same papers print
Wingen Abbott’s lettef, advising his side to shoot,
and vigorously “lay a hundred or so of the pesti-
lent marauders low,” under the simple heading of
“ correspondence.”— Bilk.
One good result of Patterson’s primage
duty was that it oast a lot of light on importers’
profits. Sewing-machines are on the free list in
Victoria, and sell at somewhere about <£lo, and it
came as a shock to the public to learn that the
average cost landed in Melbourne is not quite <£2.
Freetrade in this instance seems to run prices up
500 per shent, and people are beginning to ask if
it wouldn’t be cheaper to put a prohibitive duty
on imported machines, and then try if the same
articles can’t be locally manufactured at .£4 or so.
The old-time Liberal and Protectionist,
R. D. Reid, who ousted M'Kinley from
gilt - edged Toorak, has, besides striking a
knock-out blow to Toryism in its headquarters,
also wiped out an old personal sore. Years ago,
Reid, at the time minister, was attacked by
M’Kinley’s organ, Melbourne Punch, and that
paper’s then clever cartoons and rhymes had much
to do with “Chinaman Bob’s” political extinction.
Now, some dozen years have passed, and Reid
arises out of oblivion, shifts M'Kinley from the
legislative saddle, and rides triumphant in his
place.
The Vic. pulpits have been ringing with
exhortations to the electors to choose only “men
of character.” The clergy seem to recognise only
one kind of sin as unfitting a man for public life—
viz., sexual indiscretion. A candidate may have
been a cheating company-monger, a Stock
Exchange swindler, a cause of the ruin of thou-
sands of honest people, and the pulpits let him
alone. But if he has ever been caught winking at
a woman in an unauthorised manner, the white
chokers begin to howl. The pulpits recognise only
one kind of “ morality.” A man may even own
brothels and still be a good church elder.
Parkes’ friends are not asleep. Concur-
rently with the old man’s notice on the Federa-
tion question in N.S.W. Assembly, the other
night, the circular-letter, recounting all the good
legislative deeds he is alleged to have done, again
found its way into the hands of such of the new
members as it might be likely to influence.
Apropos, it is said that a section of the Pro-
tectionists will be quite willing to follow Parkes
(who is willing to leave the present protective
duties untouched) when he moves. They reckon
on being thus enabled to shelve Dibbs and dish
Reid in one act.
The Reform Club, another ephemeral
device of Freetraders, has been “inaugurated ” in
Melbourne, by dear old Murray Smith. Every-
thing that Mr. M. Smith touches is bound to turn
to stone, but whilst this precious club idea is in
course of fossilisation it may be as well to observe
that Sargood Softgoods figures as chief man on
the committee appointed to draw up a “ constitu-
tion,” and that this bebaubled importer is a mem-
ber of the Turner Ministry. The fact that
Sargood is brandishing a Reform Club in the face
of the Labor-party will not tend to make poor
Turner’s head lie easy in his crown.
“ We suppose that, without undue egotism,
the result may be largely ascribed to the per-
sistence with which this journal has placed the
national aspect of the case before the community”
(Melb. Argus on the result of the elections). The
supposition of the fatuous Tory journal is quite
correct. By persistently pleading for Patterson
and Freetrade it made the truculent meat-man
deader than he would naturally have become, and
induced Protectionists to vote harder than they
had intended. Without egotism, the Argus oan
take credit for having assisted the Tory side to
lose, not the other week only, but always.

by Reid that some of his friends
lately suggested a hostile hoax, and
indicated that the signatures should be
tested.

Under the State Land Bank system in
Maoriland the law-costs in connection with each
mortgage are to be^reduced to about one-fourth of
the rates now ordinarily charged. The province
is already a rather unprofitable place for lawyers,
and soon the devil’s brigade will have to take to
pick-and-shovel work.
How many alleged Labor-members of last
N.S.W. Parliament, who failed to secure re-
election, have gone back to their original occupa-
tions . Very few. But to do so would emphati-
cally be the proper caper. Say you were sent
into Parliament by the wharf-lumpers and have
now been defeated. Get up next morning, assume
the garb of toil and go to the wharf in search of a
job. You will get rough chaff from the boys as
you once more stagger under the weight of the
bags of salt. But you will prove that Parliament
hasn’t “ demoralised ” you, and when next a
member is wanted you will be that member, unless
human-nature alters within three years.
The recent debate in N.S.W, Legislative
Council on the shearing trouble was a ludicrous
spectacle. One by one the members of the House
of Kidney Fits would stagger to their feet and
asthmatically gasp about “my sheep,” “my
°bP’” “my workmen,” “my shed,” “our agree-
ment,” “my station’’—with occasional allusions
bo those “lawless brutes,” “ruffianly insurreo-
tionaries, ’ and so on. When the democratically-
inclined Greville got up to point out that the
House represented Property and Property only
he was sooled down, and when he suggested that
members didn’t care a rap for public opinion ho
was energetically hear - bear’d by Property’s
stipendiary terrier, Jacob.
The Vic. Parliamentary Labor - party,
which reckons about 16 members and gives a
general support to Turner, demanded one repre-
sentative in the new Cabinet. But the Premier;
with the fear of his aristocratic constituency
before his eyes, shudderingly refused and offered
Trenwith the Chairmanship of Committees, which
was flatly rejected. George Turner is a respect-
able solicitor to the backbone, and hasn’t sense
enough to know that it is cheaper to muzzle his
most dangerous critic with a Postmaster-General-
ship than leave him at large to grab for the
Premiership later on. The Labor-party, anyhow,
can have little sympathy with a Premier who
dares not declare war to the knife against plural
voting.
The motion, lately carried in N.S.W,
Parliament against Premier Reid, for the winding-
up of the Superannuation Fund, is not kindly
looked upon by those civil-servants bordering on
60, who expected shortly to retire on comfortable
pensions. As they have been compulsory sub-
scribers to the fund since its institution, some
nine years back, under the impression that they
would reap some such benefit, it is only fair
that they should, at least, receive a refund of
the 4 per cent, regularly “docked” from their
salaries and paid into the fraudulent fund. An
amendment on the original motion to this effect,
however, was lost. The Labor members voted for
both the motion and the amendment. And yet
the average snivel-servant would rather die, or
wear a turned-down collar, than vote Labor.
The crowd collected before the Arous
office, inspecting the Vic. general election returns
the other night, was a characteristic one, very
orderly and dreadfully distressed. No cheering
and no footing, save from the enemies of the
“cause who had failed to get within seeing
distance of the Age’s hoarding. Now and then
one heard from knots of smothered fat men vague
and despondent murmurs about “shocks to the
country’s credit ” and “capital being driven out,”
but, on the whole, the crowd at ten o’clock was as
sober as a funeral, while that before the Age was
bursting with song and turbulent with triumph.
The fronts of the two buildings, too, wore eloquent
of the truth : out of every window of the Age
office hung bunches of grinning heads, but never
a man showed himself from the Argus, barring
the artist posting the returns, and he was so in-
flicted with the prevailing gloom that a mad wag
in the crowd was moved to hand him up a bunch
of crape.

Max OTlell says that the gaining of Aus-
tralia cost England no blood, “but a deal of
whisky.” Wrong! Whisky was practically un-
known in the early days. Brandy and rum were
the tipples of our forefathers. Even on the
Palmer, 20 years ago, you never saw a bottle of
whisky.
* 9 m
Any Edwin, or Angelina, whose soul
yearneth for its affinity, can’t do better in these
dull days of matrimonial ventures than join the
Sydney Guitar and Mandoline Society. During
the brief period of its career no less than 14
members of this society have married one another.
The young man who lingers listening to a fair
damsel softly tremoloing on the poetic mandoline
is doomed.
• • •
There is a section of artists—in Tahiti—
who_ cultivate an annual custom known as a
“ private view,” which consists in throwing open
their studios just prior to the annual local art
society’s exhibition, and, after mentally deterio-
rating the visitors by libations of afternoon-tea,
there and then proceeding to explain the beauties of
their works to the helpless victims. Afterwards,
when the exhibition opens, the favoured few who
are in possession of esoteric beauties of these
pictures may be found explaining to other friends
details of “foreshortening,” “perspective,”
“atmosphere,” “tone,” &c. In fact, it is a
growing custom in that country to manufacture
public opinion about certain artists’ work, whether
it be good, bad, or indifferent, and it is sufficient
to notice this obnoxious custom in other countries
to prevent its introduction in Australia.
• • •
Dear Bulletin, —The steamers to and from
Maoriland do carry tough crowds at the present
low rates. On the Rotomahana’s last trip it was
customary for the “ push ” to rush the tables at
the first (dressing) bell, which is usually rung
15min. before meals. “Dressing,” however, was
regarded as a useless formality by the majority
who wished to get to dinner at once; so, after
“harpooning” the dessert, by way of a start,
they speedily devastated all else. One young
fellow wanted to know why the Sheol they gave
him soup in the middle of his blanky dinner.
This young man, a fine specimen physically, bound
for Coolgardie, loaded himself up with everything
in reach, and after finishing the solids he fixed his
eagle-eye on some prune-stones left on a plate by
his neighbor who had “cleared” shortly before.
These he scooped on to his own plate and pro-
ceeded to crack them with his teeth. After
assisting his jaw with a large horny hand, and
demolishing a few of them he remarked to me,
“Them’s blanky hard nuts, ain’t they mister?”
I agreed with him ; they were.—Tawhiao.

Dear Bulletin, —The professional pastry-
cook, when he makes certain varieties of cake,
always, for some occult reason or other, fastens a
paper to the bottom. Sometimes he uses a bit of
newspaper for the purpose, and then you get a lot
of valuable information along with the victuals.
The other day I came across this statement on the
bottom of a square piece of cake in a refreshment-
room : —“The deceased’s head had been nearly
severed from .... bowels protruded in various
places. A large hammer .... entrails. The
face was almost unrecognisable, and some clotted
hair . . . .” I didn’t feel as if I was taking any
cake just then, so I left. My opinion is that the
bowel and severed head business is being over-
done. —Yours, &c., T.S.

Extract from an Australian cookery-book
published in Melbourne :
I had a salmi of wild duck and a bottle of white wine
just before I sat down to write this chapter, and, behold
—what a good chapter it is I A good dinner warms the
heart, enlarges the intellect, brightens the eyes, improves
the complexion, sets a man at peace with this world, and
prepares him for the next, and conduces more than any-
thing else to promote universal good feeling. The Great
Founder of Christianity began His mission at a wedding-
breakfast and ended it at a supper with His chosen com-
panions. You cannot possibly get a better example.
In the way of cheerful egotism and unconscious
profanity even Brillat-Savarin hardly surpasses
this.

The huge crowd in front of Argus office
on election night prevented the adjacent chemist
from putting up his shutters, evidently out of
pure cussedness. The chemist scratched his head,
retired, and presently reappeared with a small
phial, from which he poured a liquid on to the
path. It was asafoetida. In five minutes the
path was clear, and at 5 a.m. next morning even
the dustman held his nose.

The much-discussed Ocean-street (Sydney)
cable-tram has to earn £lB every day to pay in-
terest on construction. The new style of grip,
which the Govt, bought for .£3OOO, has proved a
rank and expensive failure, and is so hard to work
that the gripmen are allowed a spell every third
day. Some of the men employed are, it is said,
losing weight rapidly, and several of them expect
to be in the lunatic-asylum within a few months
through sheer nerve-wear.

One amusing phase of S.A. retrenchment

The loaves and fishes protruded in Melb.
Church of England Assembly the other day.
Bishop Goe put a lot of sarcasm into his assertion
that the clergy of Melb. diocese were as good as
gold, but apt to be heavy as lead. The obvious
retort no one dared to make, but Judge Hodges
laid himself badly open to Canon Potter’s home-
thrust. Mr. Potter wanted Melb. diocese to keep
its fat things for its own clergy, and Hodges went
for freetrade, whereupon Potter applied the sauce
to the gander, wanting to know why Hodges pro-
tested against Madden being made Chief Justice ?
Whereupon Hodges fairly foamed at the mouth.
Two_ things have goaded Potter and Co.—the
appointment of a Sydney parson to replace Mr.
Perks at Richmond, and the promotion of Canon
Langley, who came from Sydney years ago, to be
archdeacon.

Advt. from Sydney Daily Telegraph :
V"OUNO English Gentleman asks those of charitable
disposition for left-oil clothing. Destitute,
0.P.0.
“Destitute” is on an “old lay.” One of the
most profitable lines of metropolitan cadging is
the gratuitous acquirement of “old do’.” The
man in the big house who gives away tailor-made
clothing that he is too particular in taste to wear
does not know the value placed upon it by second-
hand dealers. But the professional cadger knows
all about it, and will sometimes even break the
creed of his laziness by chopping up a small pile
of wood in return for some cast-off garments (and
frequently some food and money as well). This
clothing goes to the second-hand dealer for cash.
“ I’ve made 35 shilluns in one day by taking a
swell suburb,” said a loafer-about-town to a
detective quite recently.

Dear Bulletin, — What a mess was made
of the recent presentation of Humane Society’s
medals and certificates at Sydney Executive
Council room ! In the past such ceremonies have
taken place publicly at the Town Hall, but this
time the recipients were just hunted up by a
policeman, and each informed that he was
“ wanted ” at noon at the place already named.
For 35min. the three men whose “bravery and
humanity” were to be rewarded waited in an ante-
room to the Chamber, wherein sat Gov. Duff,
Premier Reid, and other members of Council. At
last, without the merest form of ceremony, the
three were called into the “presence.” The
Governor remained in his seat, and a clerk having
gabbled out the names, the medals and certificates
were handed over—without ever a word or a
shake-hands. There were no introductions, the
Councillors were discussing business all the time,
and the Governor—[ls this the courtesy N.S.W.
pays .£7OOO a year for?] —immediately got up,
and turning his back upon the company present,
began talking to Premier Reid. Which last action
went as near an insult as possible—quite enough
to make the three men wish they had never turned
up.—H.C.
« • #
Sydney has produced a prototype of
Dickens’ immortal Fagin. A pickpocket, arrested
the other day, was testified against by his pupils,
a girl of nine years and a boy of seven, to whom
he had given daily drill in the fine art of pocket-
picking. He had four “nicked” purses in his
possession, and a mild-mannered beak gave him
nine months during which to rest his hands and
practise delicacy of touch in the seclusion which
the prison grants. Apparently, however, to indent
apprentices to the pocket-picking profession is not
a crime recognisable by Law.
• « «
A poor N.S.W. country parson, after a
lifetime’s struggle with a large family and a small
stipend, got word the other day from England
that he had inherited a very big fortune. Six
days after receipt of the news he died.
Mrs. Harrison Lee told the she-males of
Adelaide W.C.T.U. that she disapproves of the
action of The Saviour in turning water into wine.
She also informed them that one step towards ex-
tinguishing the Social Evil would be to make
their children, whether boys or girls, sleep in
pyjamas. The good lady exhibited a divided-
skirt for the delectation of the meeting and asked
the men present to look the other way while she
explained how, by its use, innocent young pietists
will be able to preserve the Virtue which, in the
present style of dress, she says, is so easily spirited
away. “Oh, you naughty men!” she cried,
shaking an expostulatory finger at some of the
bald-pated parsons and other semi masculine
lovers of soft food present, “ I see you are looking
after all.” And so they were, though they pre-
tended not to be. They seemed disappointed,
too, because Mrs. Lee merely held up the garment
for inspection and did not slip off her skirts on the
stage and substitute the reformed-dress after the
fashion of Frank Thornton in “ Charley’s Aunt.”
Parson Berry delivered an address to the tabbies,
shortly afterwards, on a subject so very delicate
that allusion to it here TV absolutely impossible.'

Dear Bulletin, — Re Mrs. Besant and her
beans, doesn’t the diet of the late Mr. Nebuchad-
nezzar seem generally to lend itself to flatulence
of mind as well as of body?—to yearnings and
raauudorings which, by undue distension of the
self-consciousness, make for nebulosity of thought
and futility of action ? Fads, physical and
psychical, may come and go, but it is the omnivor-
ous man—bodily, intellectually, and morally—
who goes on for ever. Extremism, like every
abnormality, carries within itself the seeds of
reversion to the normal. The in medio maxim is
more than a mere crystal of human experience—it
is Nature’s universal lesson, writ large at every
turn.—i A. M.

book as Penholder by Pencil or Orowquill out of
Orossnib.— Bondingee.

« • •
Braidwood (N.S.W.), according to the
local paper, claims to own the smallest man on
earth, a citizen, aged 20, and 2ft. Sin. high. He
lives in seclusion on his father’s farm, and wears a
child’s petticoat, and passes, when seen at a dis-
tanoe, for an infant. A few weeks ago, he was
brought into the township and got his earliest
glimpse of civilisation. On that occasion he was
put into pants for the first time, but his modesty
insurrected against the new garment, and he
refused, with much forcible language, to appear
except in his petticoat. The spectacle of the
smallest man on earth objurgating in a short
petticoat is pathetic, with a squalid kind of pathos,
and makes one feel that the scheme of creation has
gone wrong somewhere or other, though it is
difficult to say exactly where.

Some few days ago a newsagent was
charged in a Sydney police - court with steal-
ing a bundle of newspapers. Three of the
dailies only mentioned him in a line or two,
but the S.M. Herald spread itself over the
dreary incident in half-a-column or more, and
gave every uninteresting fact i n full. The obvious
reason for all this was the careful ly-stated fact
that there were about 170 Heralds in the stolen
parcel and about 80 Telegraphs, so the incident
was an “ad.” for the circulation of the elder
organ. Then the D.T. gets level by announcing
that complaints had been received about certain
unprincipled agents who “surreptitiously” leave
the Herald at the subscriber’s door instead of the
Telegraph, and asking anyone who has been
“victimised ” by this nefarious practice to report
the same. The innuendo, of course, is that the
Herald has to be left “surreptitiously,” and
that if it were left openly there would be blood-
shed.

The Saxon and the Celt agree on one thing
—the conviction that they are immeasurably
superior to thp German. There may, however, be
seen among the Germans in South Queensland a
sight not to be witnessed among their British
fellow-colonists. A farmer builds a house and his
neighbours turn out with waggons and give him
their services gratis for the first day or two. It is
but fair to add, however, that this foolish custom
does not hold good among their native-born
descendants.

Queensland is essentially a country of
legislative anomalies and absurdities. Within the
last two months two civil servants, whose offences
were many and grievous, have been released under
the First Offenders’ Act. Now for the obverse :
A man was convicted at Rockhampton before
Judge Real of the larceny of a silver watch. True,
he had previous convictions against him, but
surely seven years was a little too Draconian, even
in Bananaland,

Australia.”

“Professional etiquette” is sustaining
some severe knocks in Sydney medical circles just
now. It is by no means uncommon for a medico

sickness, whether a brother-medico happens to be
in attendance or not.

Auckland (M.L.) Y.M.C.A. held a tearful
meeting a few days back for the purpose of slob-
bering over two young ladies who were departing
as missionaries—one to China and the other to the.
Niger region. Twenty saved Chow brethren were
on the platform, looking with the eye of the flesh
on their missionary girl, who was plump and
pleasant of aspect; and the premises were deco-
rated with streamers and Chinese mottoes. Amid
the general hysterics a large num-
ber of young women volunteered
to join the missionary move-
ment, and more are expected.
It is a curious circumstance
that the job of saving the
stolid Mongol is so larg< ly left
to the female hystericist, while
the male expounder keeps the
South Seas and other pleasant
stations mainly to himself.
There are few profitable open-
ings for trade in the land
of Joss, and there is very
little scenery ; and the society,
especially in the interior, is
limited, and the language is
difficult, and there is no land
speculation to be dene, and
the people are stony and un-
sympathetic and their desire
to kill the “ foreign devil ” is
ineradicable. Therefore the
male preacher snatches lather at
the pleasant billets in the islands,
and reaves the Chinese vineyard,
as much as possible, to the lady
disciple. As for the misguided
and over - enthusiastic lady dis
ciple, she has to take whatever
job she can get, and the rockiest,
most barren, and least profitable
corner of the vineyard generally
comes in her way. She is sent out,
with the blessing of the impressive
fathers of the church, wherever the
, heaviest spiritual shovelling is to be
I done, and there is the least chance of
getting any money or any converts, and the best
chance of being stoned by an indignant mob, or
hugged to the breast of the leper. Those males
who go with her are mostly amateur enthusiasts
like herself. The old, experienced professional
missionary knows better.

Talmage has dashed across the Australian
sky like a patent Yankee meteor, a nine-days
wonder of which the tenth day finds no trace. He
denounces drink, of course, but only for the reason
that he has found a better substitute. “When I
feel out of sorts,” he told a startled interviewer,
" when gorgons and hydras and ohimaeras come
gibbering along the nerve-tracks of this wonderful
body of mine, and variegated serpents curl and
twist along the corridors of this magnificent brain,
it is then that I call for Mrs. T. to unlock her
little cupboard and take down from the top shelf
that great modern restorative, infinitely preferable
to alcohol —the nectar of the eternal, Nature’s own
panacea, Marshall’s Tonic Nervine.

Duffy and Purves, the opposing counsel in
Speight v. Syme, bankrupted the vocabulary of
sarcasm when addressing each other while the
case was going on. But when it was finished
Duffy, Q. 0., with the beaming smile of an ogre
about to carve a plump little boy, remarked that
he “agreed with his learned friend that all the
costs awarded in this case ought to be awarded on
the higher scale.” Touching concord 1
Photo. by Talma] [Swanston-st., Melb.
Miss LEONORR OHELLI (a coming pring-donna—from
Victoria, as usual.
Miss Leonora Orelli, an Italian on the
parental side, is an Australian by virtue of having
been born in Bourke-st., Melbourne, 19 years
ago. Her infant cry soon developed musical
tendencies, and, when Miss Orelli had worked her
way along to bashful 15, she possessed a voice for
which the late Madame Chambers prophesied a
“grand future.” It is a mezzo-soprano organ of
wonderful range, but, although Miss Orelli has
made four or five appearances in public, her vocal
powers have yet to make themselves fully under-
stood. Debuting at Melb. Athenaeum Hall, some
18 months ago, the young lady took a chill, and
when she sang soon afterwards at the Exhibition
she was feeling the approach of a rheumatic fever,
which nearly robbed the world of a nightingale.
This severe illness, happily, did not impair the
patient’s voice. Miss Orelli recently sang charm-
ingly the music of Casilda, in “The Gondoliers,”
and as she is steadily returning to her former state
of robust health she will, it is expected, lose no
more time in achieving a really great reputation.

The purchaser of a sheaf of shares in an

her dresses and social “ splashes.”

Dear Bulletin, —Phil May’s sudden popu-
larity at ’Ome has stirred the Respectable press
of Australia to a state of appreciative enthusiasm
which gives the worker in art or literature “ that
tired feeling,” illustrating as it does the intellec-
tual crawlsomeness of the powers that be in the
offices of the “reputable ” organs, their incapacity
for conceiving an original opinion, and their readi-
ness to slavishly repeat the platitudes of the Hing-
lish critics once an Australian artist’s or writer’s
London success is certain. Mr. May, I know, is
not an Australian, but before coming here he was
comparatively unknown; whilst here he did a vast
amount of remarkably brilliant work, but the
“great journals,” so far as my knowledge goes,
never once ventured even to acknowledge hie
existence. After his return to England he sprang
into existence as a black-and-white artist at a
bound, and then he was discovered by the
courageous, self-reliant, daily paper editors of
Australia, and gushed over in the cant phrases of
their worshipped exemplars in the dear old mother
country. Note, too, the influence of English
theatrical criticism on the average “Australian”
critic, who invariably has the courage of the other
fellow’s conviction.—Snelu
* # *
Guy Boothby, the “great Australian
explorer,” who is credibly reported to have tra-
velled overland from Rockhampton to Bourke,
has written a book on his experiences, which are
varied and some of them very steep. The follow-
ing is his description of a ball at a Oloncurry
(Q.) hotel: —
The large dining-room was turned into a ballroom,
an accordeon supplied the music, and at least twenty
couples took the floor. As everyone knew and danced
with everybody, introductions were not needed. The
usual method of selecting the honor of a dance was to
approach the fair one and say, “Going to’ave a go-in?”
To which she would probably reply, “ My colonial 1” and
there you were! With gnat enthusiasm the ball was
kept rolling till nearly daylight, long after the accordeon
player was inebriated, and the music had dropped to
simple whistling. Bet ween the dances drinks were called
for, and not infrequently two gentlemen, having claimed
the same lady, would retire privately to decide the matter
out-ide, leaving the fair one to obtain another or await
the return of the victor, as she pleased. It was a proud
moment for her, and she invariably took advantage of it.
Our colonial oath !

The difference between a High-churchman
and a Low-churchman resolves itself considerably
into a question of choir-singing. The high shep-
herd argues that a surpliced boy, with his
thoughts wandering nowhere in particular, is less
of an irreligious influence than a female soprano,
smirking at the tenor opposite, or receiving little
messages from the baritone at the back. _ The low
shepherd is all for girls in lieu of urchins—nice,
plump, ticklesome girls, who can gather round
him on practice nights, and walk down the dark
road with him on his way home, whereby he gets
the option of resisting temptation by not kissing
the little dears. If he yields to temptation it is
just as nice to feel sorry for what he has done, so,
one way and another, the Low Church policy is
girl. The lower the church and the narrower the
chapel the more girl you invariably find ’ n h . e
foreground. Come down to the Little Bethel,
and observe a cluster of volcanic virgins singing
on the steps.

THE PSYCHIC LOVE.
Sydney.
On waxen chin
And powdered nose
Must all thy woman’s
Power repose;
With artful lip
And cheek of snow,
Whereon two blushful
Roses grow—
With grace like this
Thy lord expect
To charm, and not
With intellect.
In mind shall man
His prestige place—
While thou shalt conquer
With thy face !
W.J.
Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will he taken of communications insuffi-
ciently stamped. Name and address must
appear on MS. No liability is undertaken re
contributions voluntarily submitted, and in no
case will MS. be returned unless stamps ac-
company.
A.J.D. : “Palmistry,” as you understand the
term, is absolute imposture. The idea that anyone
should be able to say, from examination of your hand,
when you’ll get married and where you’ll travel to at a
certain age is too grotesquely absurd for any intellect
outside Callan Park, Yarra Bend, and Woogaroo.
Palmists are humbugs who live by defrauding credulous
women... Glen : A mean man, but by no means a typical
squatter. There aren’t enough of his sort to make
trouble Hess: When next Premier Reid has any dis-
putative letters to write for publication, perhaps he’ll
have them edited by yourself—or by Attorney-General
Simpson—who never errs in etiquette....W.J.T.: All
seem incredible. True they may be, but no one would
believe them,... Rose Bamboo: But, of course, we
couldn’t print it.... C. 8.: (1) No. (2) Marriage-announce-

S.S.: The case of the anti-vaccinationists stands thus: (1)
Vaccination is no preventive against smallpox ; (2) even if
vaccination is a preventive against smallpox, it is a dan-
gerous means of effecting the wholesale inoculation of
healthy subjects with blood diseases of a terrible nature ;
and (3) under no circumstances should it be made a crime
for a healthy subject to refuse to submit to vaccination,
which, like all other surgical operations, should be
entirely optional. Why, even a mangled man in a hos-
pital is generally given the choice of two alternatives.
“ Will you have the limb amputated ? If you don’t, you’ll
die.” Why not, therefore: “ Will you stand the risk of
an attack of smallpox, which may never come your way,
or will you be vaccinated and run the risk of tuberculosis,
syphilis, cancer, and so on?” C.M.: The cigar-trade

The Czar, it is cabled, has Bright’s disease.
His quiet disappearance from the globe would
probably be followed by reform. The serf eman-
cipation was intended as a prelude to a degree of
representation. There has long been a Parlia-
mentary scheme on file, and only Nihilism has
prevented its development. A majority of the
nobles desire a system of manhood suffrage, con-
fident that, as in Spain, it would be a stronghold
of the monarchy. The superstition of the
lower millions, and even the success of Toryism in
England, with a wide suffrage, re-assures them.

General Debility.
The term General Debility means a depletion (f
vital strength, a general break-up of the corporal
health, due to over-consumption and wasting away
of the life-giving constituents of the blood and
nerve tissue, really a wearing-out of the animal
structure. If your life is worth saving, it is worth
your while to treat your case thorougldy. If it is
worth your while to treat your disease at all, it is
worth your while to continue the treatment until
your health is thoroughly re-established ; you may
feel all right after the first few bottles of Clements
Tonic, so prompt are its remedial and curative
effects, but a medicine, no matter how good, cannot
banish disease in a week, the seeds of the complaint
will still remain in your system and break out
afresh, if you do not thoroughly eradicate them
Don’t forget that a relapse is twice as difficult to
cure as the original attack, so if you want to get
cured give the remedy a fair chance and recovery
is certain. Clements Tonic is a genuine article,
it is prepared for a specific purpose and contains
the material to make it an effectual remedy, and if
used faithfully it cannot fail. We do not claim
it is a cure-all, we do not say it will cure lockjaw
or bad spelling, but for all cases of impaired vital
strength it is a scientific and infallible specific.
Moreover, the cost of using it is so small you can
have a year’s course of it for less than you can
procure a week’s medical attendance, and a tenfold
better remedy than any physician can prescribe
for you. Thousands of cases have been cured by
Clements Tonic, and it will cure you if you give
it a fair chance. Read the case of Mr. James
Simmons, shearer, Junee Junction, who writes on
June 25th, 1893 Last year I was engaged
shearing on Marool, I had a severe attack of
palpitation of the heart, which rendered me at
times incapable of following my ordinary occupa-
tion. Even in the back-blocks Clements Tonic is
known. I was persuaded to try it, and by the
time I had used three bottles I was as well as ever
I had been in my life. My opinion is—it is the
best medicine in the world.—Yours sincerely,
James Simmons, shearer, Junee Junction, New
South Wales.


LYCEUM THEATRE, SYDNEY—“MA
MIE ROSETTE.”
The Sydney Morning Herald, of October Ist,
says : —“ The success of the evening was gained by
Miss Nellie Stewart, who, as Rosette, in the open-
ing of the second act interpolated Mr. Juncker’s
song ‘I Was Dreaming.’ The song was so appro-
priate to the situation, and the fair artiste delivered
the melody with such purity of tone and attractive
vocal expression that the end of the song was
marked _ by a wild demonstration of approval.
The artiste responded, and, fired by the forceful
sympathy of the audience, she excelled her pre-
vious effort. Again the audience applauded to
* an( 3, when the son# had been given for
the third time, so continuous was the enthusiasm
that Miss Stewart had to consent to a triple
encore, a circumstance that testified to the excel-
lence of the performance of Miss Stewart, and the
pleasing character of Mr. Juncker’s composition.”
