846 Macqnarie-street North, Sydney,
October 2,1894.
Mr Dear Moorabinda —
The first show held by the Art Society of
N.S.W. in the gloomy York-street rink opened
to an invitation crowd last Saturday, and
though the light might be a little better the
whole show was much more enjoyable than it
used to be when the Society’s stuffy garret in
Pitt-street, where even the paint used to melt
and no made-up woman dared show her nose for
more than five minutes at a time, was the place of
battle. 1 like a paint contest where there is one
daring daub to keep the shuttlecock of criticism
going, and Mr. “Sid.” Long’s impressionist oil,
“By Tranquil Waters,” is the sort of picture
which the “Lover of Art” and “ Constant Sub-
scriber ” are quite sure to quarrel about in the
newspapers. Mr. Long’s bold smearing will
attract everyone, but with varied effect. People
who like an artist to cross all his “ t’s,” will echo
the man who described the picture to me as
“Veal boys in a sea of ink and (it’s always the
fate of the pioneer) while the impressionable dis-
cover hidden beauties in the boy with the plum-
colored ribs, the other side will repeat the cynical
words of the unbeliever who said that though
“ Art is long, Long isn’t Art.”
Several ladies’ portraits trim the walls, and I
couldn’t help remarking that the idealised woman
is invariably depicted with most of her clothes off,
while the “ portrait of a lady ” is always dressed
to the very ear-lobes. Under these circumstances,
there is much to be said against cultivating
ideality in the chilblain season. The anaemic
“ Le Papillon ” is the picture of the idealised
female airily attired in a pair of wings and a
grappling-hook, but to my eye she looks the most
hopeless case of hip-disease that ever went head-
first into a surgical ward. Somehow or other the
woman who has her face or form painted (by some-
body else, I mean) generally is about the very last
woman who ought to have “sat,” and the cruel
part of it is that nearly every “ portrait of a lady”
is named in the catalogue. However, there are
some exceptions. The pretty wife of the Art
Society’s new president, Mrs. Reginald Bloxsome,
smiles at you out of her frame with a most natural
expression, and Mr. Tom Roberts’ “portrait of a
Sydney girl” (Miss Furlong) laughs charmingly
thro’ a bronze-colored veil. Mrs. Mary Stoddart’s
quaint portrait of little Muriel Carter struck me
as being the best and most natural piece of work
she has yet shown, and “ Master Rivers Allpress,”
by Diston —in some sort, a companion picture to
Mrs. Stoddart’s—represents with an equal merit of
simplicity a breezy-looking little fellow in a reefer
coat.
Like the rest of the world, I bow down before
Mr. Frank Mahony as an animal painter, but
when he perpetrated that unearthly pastel of the
girl with the staring, white face set in a frame of
tallowy hair, he must have been inspired thereto
by Dr. M’Carthy’s psychic photographs of
“Josephine” and “Cissie.” If I ever have my
speaking countenance immortalised, I hope it will
be done by a he-artist with a spice of gallantry at
the end of his brush, for it is evident that no dip-
lomatic woman would ever allow another woman
to draw a construction-line through her profile for
exhibition purposes. It seems to me that a woman
draws another woman too much on the unforgiving
Dutch principle, wrinkle for wrinkle. For in-
stance, one lady was painted with a shrimp-pink
nose, and when I looked up the catalogue—lo, I
found that she had been “ perpetrated ” by an
artistess.
The pictures were cleverly hung, but it was a
that put a nicely-colored piccaninny
and a half-caste in the same line with a fat white
baby. It looked like the evolution of the flabby
pale-faced infant aforesaid, which, judging by its
early avoirdupois, has already got J.P. or aider-
man written large all over it. Among the statu-
ary the only bust I was really interested in was
“The Bridegroom” (Prof. Anderson Stuart), (384),
looking very much at home in plaster of Paris
and placed back-to-back with an image of Bobbie
Burns. Even clay Scotchmen are clannish. But
I can’t think why that bridegroom was carved
with a folded sheet round his neck. It looked as
if he was getting his hair cut.
Apart from the National Gallery trustees, there
have been butt few buyers. The visit of Miss
Edith Walker, the Concord millionairess, to the
Gallery on Saturday came as a solitary ray of sun-
shine to the expectant brotherhood of the brush.
Miss Walker spent about Jll6O in half-a-dozen
pictures and, I think, got most excellent value for
her money.
I am afraid that the once Bohemian Sydney
artists are falling away into a sort of sartorial
Philistinism, or else why did so many of ’em turn
up to the private view in stove-pipe hats which
make a man look as if he went by steam ? The
modern martyr is a man in a black coat and a tall
hat, trying to gaze at a skied picture and keep his
silk hat on at the same time; and why artists, who
can, by the immemorial privilege of their profes-
sion, bo as as they like, deliberately re-
nounce their glorious prerogative of easy dressing
and try to look like so many tailor’s dummies is a
mystery to me. Julian Ashton, Full wood, and
Spence sported a becoming felt hat apiece, and
stood out from The crowd of stove-pipes who
looked like a WJie flock of Pitt-street stock-
brokenuin boom-time.
Further, even although no ladies were to be
pros,. n t. the f e |l mandate “evening dress” vm
i-i-ii. d (• >r the artists’ smoke-concert on Saturday
night. Is this one of the early effects of having a
society man—Mr. Bloxsome was once vice-regal
private secretary —for the president of the society ?
Art in a boiled shirt and a willie wagtail aggra-
vated by sixpenny boutonniere “by order of the
council I ”
The monthly re-union given by Madame Lucien
Henry at her rooms, 289 Pitt-street, last Friday
evening, went off in great style. Madame Henry,
in a handsome gown of black and old gold satin,
opened proceedings With a well-delivered lecturette
on “La Fontaine,” and then came a little farce,
“La Lettre Changee,” in which Dr. Roth as an
amorous American and Miss Hannah Ackman as
a pert soubrette displayed good histrionic ability
and a convincing French accent. Songs further
enlivened the proceedings, and dainty refresh-
ments wound up an exceedingly well-managed
entertainment. A very large audience overflowed
into the corridors.
“ Ma Mie Rosette,” which filled Lyceum, gang-
ways and all, last Saturday evening, has one
serious defect, to my mind—there should cer-
tainly be a scene of Rosette taking large bites off
a pork-pie before she falls on the straw and dreams
the flighty dream of Act 11. An innocent village-
maiden who could get in so much visionary detail
without even a spoonful of lobster-salad to help
her is simply thrown away in an operetta.
Her r6le is to pursue apocryphal males into
imaginary solitudes and electrify the Sunday
newspapers with the way these ungentlemanly
shades carried on.
Nevertheless, regarded with the dewy eye of
sentiment, my dear Rosette is a very pretty show,
and any damsel who falls a victim to a monarch’s
wiles after sitting out the second act cannot plead
ignorance of the by-law, so to speak. Nellie
Stewart is a beauteous Rosette everywhere,
but when she pops out in gloss of white satin and
glimmer of pearls, with stagey garlands of lilies
of the valley, and her hair in a coryphee crop, she
is viewed in far and away the least becoming stage-
dress I have ever seen Miss Stewart adopt. But
in the abbreviated pink garment of the first and
last acts (Nellie’s self-confessed motto is “Show
as much ankle as you like, but keep a firm grip of
your lace tucker up above ”), and in the wing-
sleeved white crepon night-dress with jewelled
girdle, she was simply adorable. By the way,
everybody expected a more or less serpentine out-
line when Nellie got the limelight behind her in
those airy robes of night, but it was evidently the
clever trick to turn in with lace petticoats and
satin shoes and stockings at the Court of Henry
IV. Rosette had her arms a great deal too
chalky in the first act—they looked like the ten-
tacles of an arsenic-chewer—and I must also rise
and object to an operatic heroine who wipes her
eyes on her hair.
Wallace Brownlow (whose huskiness is, I am
sorry to see, becoming chronic), as the festive
monarch, struck me as being somewhat too
patently wicked —one of his bold, bad glances
would put a whole nunnery on the defensive. If
Mr. Brownlow has historic precedent to go upon
when he lets everybody see that he would like to
run a harem, then it must hare been much easier
to spot a “ gallant man ” in those days than it is
now—when you have to discover him as he hands
the plate in church with a face 2ft. long, or he is
seen heading a Sunday-school pic-nic down the
harbor. Even the most up-to-date Sabbath-school
wouldn’t give a free hand to a deacon looking as
W.B. looks when Nellie’s garments flutter in the
fore-ground; and it strikes me that a gay deceiver,
who doesn’t even assume a temporary air of virtue
from time to time, as the case demands, would
only bag the birds that tumble down at a blank
cartridge. Florence Young cuts a handsome
appearance as Corisande, but she really should
keep a sharper eye on her pronunciation. “He
has given her his portrit,” said she in the midst
of a sublime hush.
Nevertheless, I like “Ma Mie Rosette,” which
is one of the best-balanced operettas I have ever
seen. The music exactly expresses the situation
at all times, and it is a pleasant thing to have a
coherent plot set before you in place of the night-
mare of burlesque. By the way, Nellie Stewart
sings “I was Dreaming” (one of H. J. Samuell’s
publications) in the spicy act, but I don’t think
the gifted Juncker would know it for his own
after Rosette has done with it, for the words and
music are ingeniously twisted to fit the operatic
situation and the prima-donna’s high O.
Sydney University may lay claim to being
roundaboutly connected by marriage with
England’s Premier, now that Professor Anderson-
Stuart has got married to Miss Dorothy Primrose
second daughter of Mr. F. A. Primrose, of Too-
woomba, Q., and cousin of Lord Ladas Rosebery
Yours faithfully, Sappho Smith.
Melbourne Confidences.
Melbourne, October 2.
In a cabled account of the Jap.-Chow sea-fight,
the Hi-Yei (Jap. training ship), which visited
Melbourne some time back, is mentioned as one
of the losses. Her officers, as some people will
remember, were nice fellows enough, though so
diminutive and juvenile in appearance as to be
known at local afternoon teas as the “ little boys
Brown ! ” Which reminds one of a chic “at
home,” given in their honor, when the daughters
of the house draped themselves in real Japanese
costumes, stuck little fans in their hair and chin-
chinned enthusiastically to the envy of girl-
friends. But embarrassment, rather than joy,
was writ large on the blushing faces of the gallant
guests. Later on, one of the mariners, a con-
scientious-looking youth, buttonholed the brother
of the masqueraders, whispering mysteriously,
“These young ladies have themselves mistaken to
wear thus garments. Such are sleeping dresses I ”
According to this, Mesdemoiselles Chrysanthe-
mum must court slumber in as high a state of
decoration as Ma Mie Rosette herself, or Mrs.
Brown-Potter’s Juliet. Imagine the feelings of
an Australian johnny, beholding on some heathen
shore the local cannibalesses got up to kill d,
VAnglaise, as they fondly imagined, in frilled
robes of Mrs. Caudle’s pattern.
The flannelette fiend has much nocturnal
hideousness to answer for, yet really, in these
burglarious days, elegance, rather than comfort,
should be a fair sleeper's sine qufl non. Not long
ago a suburban dame, cursed with a thirsty help-
mate, took refuge in a neighbor’s garden at mid-
night, where she drew the cork from her bottled-up
hysterics just outside the lady-help’s bedroom
window. The latter, clad in a scant pink robe,
and wildly pawing the air with hands encased in
black kid gloves, vamoosed her maiden-bower at a
bound and fell into the arms of paterfamilias, who
was running down the passage with the poker.
The luckless menial, condemned by day, as likely
as not, to scrub floors and black grates, was still
hoping against hope to keep her hands nice by
nightly penance, for the sake of the children’s
music and painting lessons.
But I’m forgetting the Japs. More than one
Melb. blockhead gave dire onenoe to the Hi-Yei
middies by talking in pigeon English to them.
We are as bad as our insular ancestors in this re-
spect, I’m afraid. Ages ago, a picturesque
Bananaland seaport used to carry on a cattle-trade
with New Caledonia, and one of the ships brought
back from Noumea a French husband and wife
who spoke English with quite correct fluency.
Nevertheless, the local inhabitants persisted in
addressing their visitors in a mixed dialect of
blackfellow, Chinese, and stage French. The
climax was reached when the Mayor, good plain
man, was heard remarking in a loud, explanatory
voice as he pointed to a banana or some other
tropical fruit (which, no doubt, the tourist-couple
knew quite as well as he did) —“ Carbon budgeree
—very good eat ’em—yousavee? bread-basket!”
This was too much even for the polite Frenchman,
who intimated in a well-chosen sentence that he
must draw the line at “ bread-basket ” on
Madame’s behalf.
A dejected handful of spectators, consisting
chiefly of the infants and nursemaids of neighbor-
ing professors, assembled at Wilson Hall to watch
the passing in review of a still more melancholy
group of youthful militaires, yclept the “Univer-
sity Corps.” The lads having been twitched,
squeezed, and buttoned into shape, like untidy
eight-year-olds, by their martial dry-nurse,
General Tulloch himself appeared, accompanied
by “ that sweet man ” grasswidower Penno, him
of the aesthetic handshake.
Dr. Bevan and family also arrived in the his-
toric “shay,” beaming upon all and sundry, like
the sanctified essence of Jingoism. It never
struck me before that the venerable Patriarch
models his appearance upon Dr. Dictionary
Johnson, which explains the why and wherefore
of those massy grey tresses, not at all a bad sub-
stitute for the great lexicographer’s wig. The
ordeal over, the General, artfully posing a tightly
clad and shapely leg on a higher step, addressed
the troops, laying on the jam so thickly that, in
spite of discipline, the military budlets sniggered
with self-approbation. Afterwards, however,
they had to march past a contingent of their
fellows, who, perhaps on the advice of the medical
students, administered a wholesome dose of firm,
but unkindly criticism.
Talking of “medicals,” these young gentlemen,
so frisky when well-backed up, are easily euchred
on a lone-hand. Not long ago, one of them, doing
his hospital course, was told off for the first time
to interview out-door patients. Among the
earliest arrivals was a buxom damsel, who seemed
quite at home and began by saying, “ You’ll want
to examine me, Dr 1” at the same time removing
her hat and jacket. The remark that perhaps a
vigorous inspection wouldn’t be necessary, and a
question regarding her symptoms, were brushed
aside with the statement that “Dr. Y. always did
so and so!” In spite of remonstrances, the “peel-
ing ” continued in such wholesale fashion that the
youth, now thoroughly alarmed, fled for counsel
to the veteran in charge. “Stop her, you
calf!” grunted old Sawbones. “I can’t,”
confessed the neophyte, reddening so apoplec-
tically that the chief took pity upon him,
and soon fixed things up. It’s always your old
doctor who prescribes falls off horseback for
maidenly sciatica, etc. Just as well, perhaps, as
it wouldn’t do to encourage girls like one of
Rhoda Broughton’s later heroines. This inter-
esting patient, not content with being perman-
ently bed-ridden, treasured it as her highest
ambition that she “one day might be counted
worthy of an operation !”
An orphan heiress, whose timid brother set her
a matrimonial example a few months back, has
just announced her engagement. The lucky man
—young, handsome, and possessed of a euphonious
double-barrelled name—is manager of one of the
ancestral stations. Deeply conscious of his com-
parative impecuniosity, he never told his love
until the other day, but his way of telling it at
last seems to have been a complete success.
It is whispered among the Toorak (Melb.) maids
and matrons that there is a tram-conductor on
that line who is heir to an English title and a
fortune. None of them seem to know which con-
ductor it is, but there is a manifest disposition to
“carry on ” with all the Toorak conductors indis-
criminately on the off-chance of getting the right
one. The consequence is that Toorak line con-
ductors are having a good time.
The recent sudden death of a flaxen-haired
Melbourne widow is said to have been really due
to a heart affection other than that of physical
nature. For years the lady had been the admired
of a popular city man who, at the last moment,
took to himself a much younger bride. This
decision is said to have caused painful disappoint-
ment to the elder lady, who sickened and died a
few hours after the disclosure.
Who says women can’t keep a secret? There
are a number of girls in South Melb. who call
themselves the D.M.L. Society, which organisa-
tion seems to exist solely to hold dances and pic-
nics, and, so far, no outsider has ever been able to
fathom the exact significance of the mystic letters.
Perhaps they mean “Don’t Marry Lata”
The latest bazaar notion is a signature-quilt,
upon which anyone can have a facsimile of his
signature embroidered at a cost of one shilling.
A unique specimen has the names of ordinary
mortals grouped round an emblazoned centrepiece,
which includes, among others, the autographs of
Talmage, evangelist M'Neill, Robert Reid, Mayor
Snowden, and Premier Reid (N.S.W.). The first-
named celebrity, when timidly requested to “sign
his name in a good cause,” fingered the provided
material (sateen) disparagingly, and made it evi-
dent that he didn’t consider the background rich
and rare enough for so illustrious an inscription.
Sassiety wonders how Westralia will suit ex-
Seoretary Ralston. It was Kismet, no doubt,
that he and his liege lord should part; but why
did the lot fall on the only member of the Hope-
toun suite with any looks to lose—those regions, by
all accounts, being ruinous to the complexion ? A 1
Victorian on a station over there, in a district
compared with which Coolgardie is damp, in-
dulges, nevertheless, in a daily wash, as he would
contrive to do were he banished to Dante’s
Inferno. But he atones for the extravagance by
conserving the water after use, and doling it out
to his blackboys to make their tea with.
A recent Salon lecture on life in North Queens-
land 20 years ago was illustrated by limelight
views, which couldn’t have played down to the
masses more candidly had the entertainment been
a Snazelle rational Sunday. The lecturer himself
almost invariably occupied a strong position in the
centre of his lantern-slides. He was depicted in
bright reds and blues grasping a revolver or two
in each hand, while tribes of ferocious blacks prac-
tised throwing the javelin at his dauntless heart.
When there were not natives to be held at bay
there were alligators to be driven back. Any pid.
tures which did not represent adventures of this
sort erred upon the side of being still awfuller
One was entitled “The blood-stained tent,” an*
other the “Cannibal camp.” It is needless to
describe them, but even Budge and Toddy couldn’t
have sighed for anything “bluggier.” Not that
it wasn’t amusing and interesting, though hardly
what one expects in the very stronghold of female
intellect.
Hand-shakes become more and more gymnastic.
At present everyone, not hopelessly homespun
rises on their toes in unison with the sweeping
arm curve. The effect aimed at is not a jerk nor
a bounce, but an almost imperceptible rise, fall
and rebound, like an inflated penny balloon bob-
bing very gently against a wall.
Ricordi and Co., of New Babylon, have sent me
half-a-dozen new songs of the love and longing
kind, all of them built for tenor or soprano, but
best adapted to the male darling, “ For the last
last time,” by Milton Wellings, is a pretty enough
composition, which gives the singer an oppor-
tunity to mention a heart throbbing loud with
Passion’s pain-capital P for Passion. Perhaps
the most fetching of the lot is “ In the Hush of
the Night,” a languorous Tosti ditty, words by
Frank Weatherly, A soulful drawing-room tenor,
singing this under circumstances favourable to a
mash, would be a very dangerous person, and I
don’t like to recommend it on that account
Filippo Filippi’s “O, Tell Me Why !” is a grace-
ful song, suited to any respectable soprano who
having been lately jilted by the boy of her heart’,
would like to know his reason for treating her
thus.
VAN M.
According to cablegrams, the Czar spends
his days in kidney-disease and his nights in weep-
ing. The parsoni cal press moralises painfully
over the common lot; and, in quoting the pathetic
lie that when the “Little Father!” heard bad
news of his consumptive son he cried, “God!
What have I done to be so severely punished ?”
gives no hint of the tortured victims of tyranny—
the executions, the floggings; the women stripped,
chained, knouted and outraged ; the quicksilver
mines, the Siberian hell, the choking of Liberty
with her own blood. There is nothing pathetic
about the agonies of mere common people, whose
fathers never robbed and murdered them into a
throne.
A. J. Thynne, the Q. delegate to the
Canadian pic-nic, makes out that barren festival
to have been even more useless than The Bulletin
considered it. He points out that the whole
thing was regarded in Canada as a dodge of the
Conservative Govt, to win votes for the coming
election •, that the Canadian newspapers reported
it in six-line pars., sprinkled among “street
brawls and suicides that Wilfred Laurier, the
brilliant leader of the Canadian Opposition, was
ignored all through; and that the whole thing
was a hole-and-corner conclave, debating in
secret, disagreeing in public, and ending in smoke.
The delegates discussed whether the German lady
who is titular head of the British Empire should
be called in future “Victoria, Queen of Great
Britain, Canada and Australia, and Empress of
India.” They did absolutely nothing else. Bub
Thynne, Suttor and Co. have had a marvellously
good time and oceans of champagne, and it only
remains for the taxpaying ass to rise and foot the
bills, as usual.
Dear Bulletin, — Your comments on the
breach between Q. Parliamentary Labour-party
and Opposition hardly do justice to the Labour
men, who object to help to quench the Opposi-
tionists’ ancient thirst for portfolios. Powers,
Drake, and Groom were much disappointed at nob
being included in the first callous Coalition
Government, and Drake was openly charged
recently by the Ool. Sec. with being anxious for a
portfolio in the existing “peculiarly vicious and
corrupt ring of public plunderers.” Powers is a
lover to this day of Kanaka labor, which he
believes is essential to the success of the sugar-
industry. He also believes in the principle under-
lying what you designated “ the biggest steal on
earth ” —the last Land - grab Railway scheme.
Powers only dissented from details, not from
the principle. Groom, in Parliament, is the
shiftiest politician in Queensland. In his own
paper, and on the public platform, but especially
in private conversation—" in strict confidence,
mind you”—he is an able, sturdy democrat.
Fogarty, another Oppositionist and senior mem-
ber for the same constituency as Groom, is
Groom’sman-Friday—apolitical apprentice learn-
ing his trade. Thorn, who succeeded Lovejoy for
Aubigny this year, was returned in the teeth of
Groom’s paper’s opposition—which has badly
soured the temper of the “Father of the House/’
Yet another remnant of the Griffith gang
was a adherent of the “ shuffler from
Wales.” He fished unsuccessfully for the Govern-
ment-printership, and never quarrels with the
off-chance of a billet. The remaining three or four
who professed to follow Powers, voted so con-
sistently with the Govt, that the only apparent
reasons why they sit on the Speaker’s left
are, first, because the Government bitterly
opposed Central Separation ; and, second, because
here is nob convenient accommodation on the
other side. _ The division-lists last session, and still
more for this one, show that the alleged Opposition
vote with the Government with sickening monotony.
The principal cause of Powers’ resignation was
undoubtedly the solid vote of the Labor 17 in five
divisions on the estimates three weeks ago, in
favor of the reduction of the high salaries. These
commenced with the Government House expenses
and included a reduction of pay for the
sinecure office of President of the Legislative
Council; also, the application of the One-man-
one-billet principle to the Clerk of the Assembly,
who is also secretary to the Board of Waterworks.
Not one' of the Opposition voted for these
reductions. When Hoolan held the Labor-party
leadership he took the honor very easy, and never
once made his power felt. Powers liked this
arrangement immensely, and as he played the
part of the Opposition in orthodox fashion the
Government also liked the “compact,” because
Powers having declared his intention of “nob
offering any factious opposition,” MT1 wraith
got on swimmingly. Hoolan would mix drinks
with the Government men—so he was a jolly good
follow. Glassey will nob even talk to them outside
formal matters, and always treats them as public
enemies. That is why Glassey is not liked.—
Toon, Beeswing.

Dear Bdllktin, —You say Giffen has a real
grievance with the Norwoods because they shut him out
of the Association, thus excluding him from the Cricket
Council. “ Considering what he has done for S.A.
cricket" Is all very well —but how about considering
what ids execrable temper has undone for Australian
cricket ? F.
Editor Bulletin,—** Herod ” objects to the
10 per cent, totalisator commission. He reckons this per-
centage big enough to kill betting. The machine kept
£990 at Adelaide races on the day he takes—does he
reckon that the bookmakers would have netted less?
With the totalisator 90 per cent, of the investments
always goes back to the public—but with the bookmaker
when the merry outsider catches the judge’s eye that 90
per cent, stays in the bag. It may be that the machine
spoils racing, and the spoilt racing may stop betting—but
that is not the fa" ■ f the percentage. It is because the
machine is honesi. a... horse-owners can't combine into
gwindiicates with it teat racing is discour ged. And
whose loss is that?—F.^
In the last Surrey-Lancashire match, which
ended in a tie, Richardson left a bruise on every one of
the Lancashire batsmen although the wicket was fairly
soft. Little Briggs got two successive boosters on the
thigh, another on the small of the back, and a flick on the
ear from a “ lightning delivery” that rose to the occasion.
The curious thing is that bulky W. G. Grace, who stands
well in front of his wicket, never gets maimed by the fast
bowiers. ,
Paddy M'Gowan’a funeral was three times as
long as Margin Loughlin’s.
The Hayes-Australasian case brought to light
a lot of the cunning of the clever jockey-boy. And they
are all alike. One of them, the son of a well-known
trainer, was given £6 by a bookmaker to ride a good and
fit horse “stiff,” but just before the race the boy
entrusted the “fiver ” to one of his juvenile mates to put
it on his (the jockey’s) mount. “You go to ,”
mentioning the bookie who had given him the money,
“ he’ll give you the longest odds.” The little deal gave
him a wager of £3O to nothing, which he won. But what
a have for Aaron 1

Whakawatea (the name borne by Hungerford’s
horse) means in Maori what Faugh-a-ballagh means in
Irish—“ Clear the way."
• * • *
Dan O'Brien reckons to win the Caulfield and
Melbourne double with Launceston and Loyalty.
* * • •
A peculiar treble for the three Cups—Caulfield,
Solon : New Zealand, Solano ; Melbourne, Solanum.
• • • •
The death of the stallion, Oap-Apie, in Maori-
-1 md, revives an old yarn about the true pedigree of Car-
tune (Musket-Mersey). There is a man living at Otaki
( Vellington) who mysteriously hints that he knows all
about it, but stat ements of this character have to be taken
with a grain of salt, and are generally made over a pot of
beer. There is not much doubt about Musket being Car-
bine’s sire. Cap-k-pie sired a few fair horses, including
the champion one-eyed gunner, Despised.
* * • *
Pinrose (by the Musket horse, Hiko), who is
being backed for the New Zealand Cup, is, if anything,
smaller than Paris.
• * • *
Norton, Maoriland’s champion ’chaser, who is
coming to Australia, used to run in small hack-races
across the water at one time, and is a specimen of thp
“ hacks ” they breed in the North Island. A “ colonial ’*
pradin the next Liverpool Grand National will be some-
thing new, and the wealthy Gollan means it. He was
offered the boss hurdler, Liberator, for the same price as
he paid for Norton (£500) just before he went out and
won the Q.N. Hurdles at Christchurch, but he didn’t take
the owner at his word.
• • • •
The unpleasant Blackham-Tooher incident is
likely to be revived during the coming cricket-season.
The only way out of the difficulty is a full apology from
Blackham, and, considering that his playing-days are
over, he would be wise to back down.
• • • •
The revival in professional running will pro-
bably interfere with the amateur movement lately grow-
ing. Like Gordon’s “Mr.” Dick Neville, young Aus-
tralia likes to go “ for the pluuder.”
• • • *
Another athletic club is mooted in Melb., with
Prof. Miller as the central figure.

Moule, the solicitor who beat ex-Speaker Bent
for Brighton (Vic.), was, perhaps, the least popular Aus-
tralian cricketer who ever visited England, not even bar-
ring Giffen. , * ,
The Pattersonian candidate for a fashionable
Vic. constituency won a pot over Projectile’s win. Tht
numerous bank-clerks of his committee stood in, and by
squaring up shortages will be able to stave off the inevit-
able Coburg.

The A. J.S. Bank, Sydney, once prided itself on
possessing cracks who buttressed N.S.W. in the cricket-
field. Percy M'Donnell, now in Brisbane, runs a hides-
and-skins business for the Graziers’ Co.—whose manager
is a cricket enthusiast. Ferris threw over his native heath
for an alluring billet in a Bristol bank so as to play for
Gloucester in county cricket; and his bowling comrade
Turner (“ the terror ”) is no longer an officer of the A. J.S.

A queer mistake was made at Port Adelaide
(S.A.) races Sep. 22. The totalisator windows are closed
when the gong is sounded announcing the start. ,For
some reason the gong did not sound in one race, and the
tote officials went on receiving money, in fact, several
cute sports actually planked their money on the winner
after the race was over and received a£3 Ss. div.l Even-
tually a pressman went and sounded the gong or those
officials would be receiving money now.

Some rich “mugs,” smart enough at their own
businesses, to whiph they ought to stiok, lost between
them at least £20,000 at the two most recent Randwick
meetings.

Following the example of the V.R.0., the
A.J.C. has resolved to give all the starting-machines a
trial before making a final selection. Gray’s worked well
at the Spring meeting. At next meeting a show will be
given to Tom Power’s, of S.M. Herald.

Harry Evans, the cue-ist, wants to play Mem-
mott again, and in explanation of his former defeat
alleges that the table was tampered with.

monopolising all the ante-post betting, especially in
respect to country clients.
Sydney Tatt.’s Club is considering whether it
would be advisable to reduce the present prohibitive
entrance lee of £250.

An Auckland (M.L.) owner, who used to race a
mare named Eve, has now three oolts in his stable called

A valuable foal just dropped by Springtime
(dam of Harvester) to Trenton was lately stolen from Mr.
S. G. Cook’s paddocks at Loch (N.S.W.). Well, the
stealer daren’t proclaim the pedigree, and a racehorse is
not much without one unless he proves a good performer.
By the way, we have heard nothing of the mysteriously-
bred gallopers that have been expected around Mudgee
since Cranbrook was “ loaned” for a few weeks.
••• * ■
Q. Assembly having declined by 28 votes to 9 to
endorse the official order refusing transmission of ‘ ‘ sweep”
letters, Tattersall’s voluminous correspondence to Bris-
bane is to continue as usual. The Government is said to
contemplate comprehensive anti-gambling legislation. If
the Government is wise, it will attempt only to regulate,
not to suppress, the gambling spirit. But Q. Govern-
ment is merely discreet, and will probably, in its own
Interests, leave the question alone altogether.
• * * •
At the annual regatta of N.S.W. Rowing Asso-
ciation on Saturday, the North Shore R.C., as per usual,
wou the Senior Eights, but it did not prove the walk-over
North Shore got used to last season, as the S.R.O. pulled
a remarkably good race.
As might have been expected, Fitzsimmons
made short work of Dan Creedon in their recent meeting.
The cutty middleweight of the Creedon stamp has no
show with the new order of lengthy glove-fighter. When
Fogarty met Choynski in Sydney, the former thought he
had a show of getting close enough to land an auctioneer
of the sort which broke Charley Dunn’s massive jaw, but
he soon found the fallacy of any such idea, and very
sensibly threw up the game, telling the onlookers that it
was only foolishness to stand and be chopped to pieces.
* ♦ ♦ •
The chief feature, as affecting future events, of
Sydney Tattersall’s much-postponed Spring meeting, at
Bandwick, last Saturday, was Ruenalf’s win of the Spring
Handicap, which he took easily from Escapade ; and as it
is said Keys had half of Xattersall’s sweep-money, he must
have had a good win.

It would about break Tom Payton's heart
should Warpaint pip one of the syndicate’s neddies in the
Caulfield Cup.

H. Breson won the 100 miles Road Race in
Melbourne on Saturday in 7h. 42min. 7seo. He won tha
100 miles race on the previous Saturday.

Before the leaders of the Turner party
were “sent for” by Gov. Hopetoun, they were
commanded to the sanctum of David Syme. The
Turner Government will be virtually under the
thumb of the Age. It was so with the Shiels
Ministry.
Even some of the friends of the defendant
in a big Australian law-suit are astonished at
the flagrant one-sidedness of the judge’s summing-
up, and there is talk amongst the legal profession
of a public indignation meeting being called to
protest against a judge practically taking an unpaid
brief for one of the parties to the suit which he is
trying.
Nothing can touch Cooper’s sheep-dip on
the show ground. At the big Chicago Fair, sheep
treated with this famous dip secured 330 awards;
at the Australasian Sheep Breeders’ 1894 show,
and at the British Royal Agricultural 1894 show
they were no less successful; and at Sydney 1894
Tasmanian stud sales over four-fifths of the sheep
catalogued were Cooper-dipped. Harrison, Jones
and Devlin are agents.
The Apex keyless watch, made at the
Beaucourt factory in France, has entered the
Australian market in earnest, and is-advertised at
igl post free to any address in Australasia —vide
page 18 of this week’s Bulletin. It is a stem
short-winder, has works equal to those of the best
.£8 silver or £2O gold watch, is set in an untarn-
ishable case, will stand the roughest usage, and is
altogether the cheapest, most reliable and best
popular watch ever made. The Beaucourt Watch
Co., Box, No, 686, Gener.J Post-office, Sydney,
will be glad to receive auu publish testimonials
from Australian buyers.
For the last five years the Sydney printing-
trade has been “ lower than ever.” Exactly how
much deeper into the mud it is going to get
nobody knows ; but if it gets any further there
may be “printing,” but certainly nothing worth
calling “ trade.” Things are cut very fine nowa-
days. Keen competition has made the profit
margin so small that you can —puff ! blow it
away, almost. Customers know their power, and
take a merciless advantage. It is quite ordinal V
fora dozen firms to be asked to tender for a jou
like 100 ball-programmes, worth about 25s. —“and
send a good lot of samples, mind !” And the
wretched firms do it. They have to; and they go
about on tenterhooks until the “committee ” has
decided “to give it ” —to the other fellow. Then
they console themselves by making out an esti-
mate for 1000 Bvo. circulars for somebody else.
But they have their revenge on the printers’ fur-
nisher. Crows on a slaughter-yard fence are mild
representatives of the furnishing travellers when
word goes round that So-and-so wants half -a-reamof
duplex. In the old days you couldn’t get half-reams.
Now you can get quarter-reams, quires, anything.
In fact, if it’s only a small job, the plan is to get
a dozen sample sheets from each of half-a-dozen
firms, and work the job on those. And, then, if
you want a good new line of type, and don’t care
to buy the font, you can always borrow it. So
there are compensations. The masters say the
compensations don’t go far enough, and have just
reduced jobbing hands’ rates from £3 to £2 12s.
for 48 hours. The £3 has only been earned as a
minimum during the last half-dozen years ; before
that it was £2 15s. The men offer to take £2
165., which is little enough to keep a family on ;
but the masters are desperate, and there has been
one lively lock-out already. Happy the score
or so at the top of the trade who will get their £3
whatever happens.

Editor Bulletin. —Will the proposed Queens-
land £lO stallion tax tend to improve the Dreed of horses?
It will not affect the light, weedy gallopers that stand at
high fees to supply the demands of the racecourse ; but
the powerful, slow horses which, because they hive not
speed enough to succeed on the turf, are sent early to
the stud before their legs are knocked to pieces or their
wind is hopelessly gone, will be lost as sires. Not being
performers, their services will not fetch more than 30s.
per mare, and it will not pay with the tax. Yet they
make the best sires for useful horses. What is wanted is
common sense in breeding. I once knew a squatter whose
ideal of a useful horse was a cross between a draught
mare and a Welsh pony. It gave substance and activity.
Of course, it did when it turned out right; otherwise a
mongrel. —Eqdks.
• * • •
Why do the sporting scribes bore us through the
winter with their tiresome “ visits to the training-stables,”
so insipid since poor “ Augur ” retired from the business?
Well, it enables them when a despised outsider, never
mentioned in their criticism, gets home, to say: “ When
I saw him as a foal, two years ago, I wrote that the little
fellow would yet win a race if he kept on growing the
right way,”
• • • •
House-to-house bookmaking is the latest
Southern profession. Soon the Melb. householder will be
awakened from his nice, warm, calm slumber by Bridget’s
knuckles on the door. “ Plaze, sorr, it’s Mr. Isaacs, and
he wants to know who ye’ll back ter day for Moonee
Valley.” The idea is a taking one and pregnant with
possibilities.
• • • •
There is a lot of senseless hate displayed by one
Australian paper towards Corbett. Among other names,
it calls him a “ coward.” Well, he faced and beat Sulli-
van and Mitchell, the only other white man plucky
enough to face the Boston slugger in the zenith of his
fame—or infamy. He also faced Jackson without defeat,
and time and circ 'instances have since favored him. It
augurs ill for poor Peter when the people, who predicted
that he must go down before Slaviu, now “ barrack” for
him.
• • « *
Edison proposes, by means of the kinetoscope,
to record every movement of the Corbett-Jackson fight—
when it comes off.
• • • •
Apropos Matt Hardy, the old png., lately in Bal-
larat Benevolent Asylum. Here are the fates of some of
his contemporaries of the ring. Joe Kitchen died in the
Alfred Hospital, Sydney; Harry Sahara “ pegged out ”in
Melb. Benevolent Asylum ; Black Perry died in Melb.
Gaol. Alf. M‘Larencommittedsuicide with £lO in his pocket
on a voyage from Melbourne to Sydney. Bill Eccleston
died in Pentridge prison. Bob M'Laren, champion of
California, &0., died in his pub., the Glasgow Arms, Main
Road, Ballarat, but is buried, at his own request, in Melb.
general cemetery. Tom Curran is understood to be alive
keeping a pub. in Gippsland, and “ Bill ” Branch died at
Elizabeth Bay, Sydney, a wealthy bookmaker and race-
horsj owner.
• * • •
Why should not “Ted.” Evans have an honorary
life-member’s ticket for the N.S.W. Cricket Association’s
ground? Has not he done quite as much for Australian
cricket as Banuerman, Coates, Garrett and Co.? Mr.
Evans was lately retrenched from the Civil Service and is
now, probably, a poor man.
• • • *
A ragged, miserable-looking, and totally desti-
tute man was arrested lately in Auckland (M.L.) Public
Library for mutilating a newspaper file. The first im-
pression was that the poor wretch had been cutting out
an “ad.” relating to a vacant situation, but he hadn’t;
the stolen bit of paper contained the Melb. Cup betting
quotations. The officials were inclined at first to take
strong measures, but there was something so incongruous
about this tattered and bony object going round feebly
on two emaciated feet in search of betting quotations
that they set him down at last as a monomaniac, and
only ran him out.
• • • •
The Royal Humane Society lately presented
two medals in Tasmania. One, to a laborer for gallantly
saving life ; the other to a schoolboy lor proficiency in
swimming exercises with reference to life - saving.
Apparently the same class of medal and certificate
was given in each case. What possible analogy justifies
this? In so vulgarising its badges, the Society does its
best to make them valueless.
• • • •
Spofforth now occasionally plays for Hampstead,
and is qualified for Middlesex, though he is never called
upon to take a place in the county eleven. They say his
bowling is affected by a certain action of his elbow, which
destroys his accuracy. He is still irresistible against
second-class batting, and recently got seven wickets for
as many runs.
• • * *
Dear Bulletin, —Football ? Why not feetball ?
The bladdery thing is kicked by several feet at once often
enough. And everybody kicks it with both feet, any-
how.—Dipso.
• • • *
In Sydney, pheasants are selling at 12s. 6d. a
pair. In most parts of Maoriland they can be shot off
country roads-in season. Out of season they are
“ knocked over accidentally, yer Wushup.”
• • • «
Te Whiti, the colt by Malua—Fortuneteller, is
fairly well named. He is called after the Maoriland
n Give prophet who raised Cain at Parihaka a long time
ago, and had to go to gaol for not paying his grocer's bill.
• • • •
A few weeks ago a N.Q. parson, while ringing a
church bell, had his brain concussed by the falling of the
clapper. The other day a fire occurred suddenly in the
billiard-room of the Victoria (sporting)Club, Melbourne;
but the flames melted a water-pipe which effectually
quenched them. The odds are always in favour of the
bookmakers ; even the Fates “ hedge " with them.

On the day that the now-defunct Maxim was
sold in Auckland (M.L.) for 220 gs. that frost, Enfilade,
t alised 850 gs. Maxim was only beaten twice—by Sextant
Jat Christchurch), and by Abercorn. Record: eight wins,
ten starts.

Few owners are happy in horse-nomenclature.
Captain Machell is an exception. He has just named an
animal, by Chillington out of Discovery, “ North Pole.”
• • • *
A sign of the times :—On previous occasions
the N.S.W. Railway Commissioners ran special first-class
trams to Rand wick races-fare 3s. fid. return. At the
recent A.J.C. Meeting, though the fare was reduced to
2s. fid. the trams were not a quarter filled.

In August last two horses only turned out for a
mile race at Paisley (Scotland)—Halsbury and Pippin ;
i'ol was laid on Halsbury, but a dead-heat resulted. In
th • run-off “the talent” laid 7 to 2 on Halsbury, and
o <e more they dead-heated. The next lime 7to 4 was
laid on Halsbury, and he was beaten by the shortest of
beads. Infatuated talent.

The Caligraph Writing Machine.
THE CALIGRAPH embodies all the highest
developments of applied mechanics, and its
principle having stood the test of 15 years, its
success towers above the fantastic devices of mush-
room competitors. ’■ ■'
“I like Caligraph writing better than that of
any other typewriter because the type comes out
so sharply and clearly.” The prominent Anglican
clergyman, who used these words, had hit upon
the chief characteristic of the Caligraph without
exactly knowing what brought it about. The
result is attributable to the fact that the platen of
the Caligraph being bevelled the type strike
against a flat surface thus producing a full and
even impression. When you see typewriting with
only the middle of the letters clear you know that
the machine used was not a Caligraph, but one
fitted with the ordinary round platen. When yon
read a typewritten letter, with some of the type
leaning to the right and the rest to the left, yon
may be sure that your correspondent has a crude
machine, with curved typebars, which naturally
become more crooked, and thus twist their typ ■
out of perpendicular. Then remember that Urn
Caligraph has straight typebars which, like every
thing else in the machine, are fitted with adjust-
The Caligraph manifolds 15 carbon copies at
one operation, and used with the Edison Mimeo-
graph reduplicates 1500 copies from one stencil.

NEY
Round the Fire.
For The Bulletin.!
When the camp-fire leaps before us
Red and restless to the sky,
When the horse-bells chime in chorus
As the hobbled nags go by—
When the slanting moonbeams glisten
Down the grass-blades dewy-damp,
Then we lie awake and listen
To the strange tales of the camp.
Tales of the muster and tales of the mine,
Hunger and thirst on a Western track ;
Tales of horses and work and wine,
Danger and death in the far “out-back.”
Of a woman’s smile or a woman’s frown,
Of fortune scattered or fortune won;
Tales that will never be written down
So long as the seething world goes on.
Heap the blazing logs together,
Watch the red sparks climbing higher,
Scorn of Fate and scorn of weather
Holds the circle of the fire.
While the flames sheet wild and wide
Laugh and revel, one and all ;
Time to lay the mask aside
When the dead, grey ashes fall.
Tales of the sea and full tide on the bars,
Tales that are light with the lift of the brine,
Till the moan of the rigging, the plaint of the spars,
Is born of the night wind that rustles the pine—
Of a land that is further than all the seas,
Of a land that is ours by the fetter of blood;
And we lie and listen, and off the trees
We catch the snows in the moonlight flood.
Heap the blazing logs together.
Watch the red sparks climbing higher,
Scorn of Fate and scorn of weather
Holds the circle of the fire.
While the flames sheet wild and wide
Laugh and revel, one and all ;
Time to lay the mask aside
When the dead, grey, ashes fall.
GLENROWAN.
A Mauritius man recently received an
application for payment of a debt equivalent to
about Is. 2d., owing for repairs to a saddle., This
was the letter : —
Honored Sir,—I approach the throne of your honor’s
greatness and learning in the hopefulness that your belly
will yearn when he learns of myself, O, misfortune. Since
your honor’s greatness and glory have gone up there is
for me little works. Pultun sahibs (officers) and those of
police say to this poor damnable, "Go be dam.” YoUr
worship will thus know how I am poor, there coining no
works to me. Ever are my intestines void, and my wile
and female offsprings are laughstocked by all neighbor,
which your lordship may prevent by timely payment of
debt, viz., rupees one, annas eleven (I rupee II annas),
for which act of mercy and loving kindness like to that of
the Lord in the holly Bible, your humble petitioner will
day and night ever pray world without end for your
greatness and your lordships posthumous and spare
progeneys, and this dammed poor man will ever glory
hallilujah.
Ex.
The successful removal of Piles of the
most stubborn description is being effected by
Sharp’s new “Cannalin” treatment, large quan-
tities of which are being forwarded to sufferers
throughout the colonies. It is obtainable, with
advice, from 257 George-street, Sydney.
Tire good old copy-book theory that success
is the reward not of luck but of perseverance,
industry, and genius, gets some hard knocks now
and then. James Munro, ex-Premier, ex-Agent-
General, and ex-paper-millionaire, now potters
away in a little wooden office in a Melbourne
suburb selling £lO allotments and letting four-
roomed wooden cottages. He probably works
harder than he ever did before, and he has all the
brain he ever had, which isn’t much, but it does
him no good. His luck is gone, and it was all
he had to depend on. Matthew Davies is a rather
small and very ordinary solicitor now, and
from present appearances he will be a smaller
and even more ordinary solicitor by-and-bye.
About the brainiest financier ever known in N.S.
Wales now makes a precarious existence, in the
prime of his life, by doing odd jobs on the
wharves. He has six times the intellect of Munro
and Davies rolled together at their best, and the
only thing he wants is luck. But his mana has
departed, and his “ mozzle ”is out. The man
whose mozzle is out may have all the financial
capacity of all the Rothschilds and yet sell
vegetables on a barrow, and not have enough
spare cash to buy grease for the front wheel. And
the man whose mozzle is in may have no brains
worth speaking of, and yet may start 18 banks,
without any capital, and have a chance to retire
with a million if he only has enough sense to
seize it.

The Australian Cassowary .
The first cassowary described in Australia was
shot by a N.S.W. aboriginal (Galmarra, hailing
from Patrick’s Plains), who accompanied Ken-
nedy’s memorable expedition from Rockingham
Bay to Cape York in 1848.
In 1857, the first cassowary brought to Sydney
was landed there by Captain Devlin, of the cutter
Oberon. The bird came from the island of New
Britain, where the natives called it “Moorook.’’
The Australian cassowary is found only in
North Queensland on the east coast from the
Cardwell Range to Cape York. The Cardwell
blacks say the cassowary has rambled farther
south since the advent of the white man, and a
solitary bird may have even wandered along the
coast range, and through the scrubs of the Burde-
kin to within 12 miles of Charters Towers,
impelled by the same restless spirit that sent
Port Essington buffalo to the plains of the
Flinders. The cassowary is an inhabitant of the
dense tropical jungle that clothes from base to
summit the coast ranges of the Cape York Penin-
sula. This jungle also covers the level country
between the mountains and the seabeach from
Cardwell to Cooktown. It crosses the range and
extends, in places, for some distance on the
western slopes. The cassowary follows that
jungle to its outer edges, east and west. North
of Cooktown it inhabits the belts of jungle on the
east coast from Princess Charlotte Bay to New-
castle Bay. A full-grown cassowary stands from
five to six feet in height, and weighs from 150 to
2001bs. One shot by my son during the Bellenden-
Ker expedition, in 1889, and weighed in presence
of the Colonial Botanist, was 1841bs, This bird
is now in the Queensland Museum. The second
bird in the Museum was shot by me on the Barron
River, and weighed 1701 bs. One shot by John
Nairne on Fresh water Creek, near Cairns, weighed
2501bs., and I have seen specimens that must
have stood six feet six inches and weighed nearly
aooibs.
So large a bird requires an enormous quantity
of food, and a full-grown one must eat at least a
hundred pounds of fruit daily. Wild fruits of
many kinds grow in great abundance in the
scrubs inhabited by the cassowary. Every
bird has a favorite feeding - ground, and
comes year after year to some particular tree
or trees, generally the oil nut, pencil cedar, Om-
phaleus Cryptocarpus, or Davidsonian Plum. The
cassowary is also fond of the Burdekin Plum,
which grows alike on good soil and the open forest
sandy belts near the seabeach. About 20 different
fruits are eaten by the cassowaries on the Russell,
Mulgrave, Barron, and Daintree rivers. In cap-
tivity they are very impartial in their diet, display-
ing uncontrolled omnivorous propensities, and
swallowing with equal satisfaction boiled sweet
potatoes, raw liver, fresh eggs, dead rats, and
glass marbles. I never knew anything seriously
disorganise the epigastric region of a cassowary
except a tin of red lead. The digesting apparatus,
which yields excellent pepsine, is a large digestive
sac, not the ordinary gizzard of a bird. Connected
with this sac is a single large intestinal canal like
that of a tiger, and the digesting process is
effected with astonishing rapidity. All food is
imperfectly assimilated, the harder fruits occasion-
ally showing very trifling changes. Though
chiefly frugivorous, the wild cassowary also eats
beetles, rats, mice, young birds, tender bulbous
roots, and small fish stranded in floods or left in
evaporated pools. If man ever acquires the
appetite and digestion of the cassowary, the whole
human race will soon be exterminated by famine.
Even the best stuffed specimen gives but a poor
representation of this magnificent bird. The taxi-
dermist has, so far, failed to reproduce the
beautiful scarlet and orange colors, and marvel-
lous opalescent shades of light and dark blue on
the head and neck. Absent also are the proud,
imperious carriage, the measured, stately stride,
the sonorous ventriloquial voice, and the deep,
dusk fire of the dark, lustrous, malign eyes. The
two long wattles on the throat are found on both
male and female. Some are destitute of this
appendage, like one of those in our museum. On
the top of the head is a ridge-shaped helmet
about four or five inches in height, one of Nature’s
wonderful adaptations to the necessities of environ-
ment. Without that helmet a cassowary would
have to live in the open forest, for the lawyer vine
of the jungle would tear his head and neck to
pieces the first time he ran from an enemy. When
running in the dense scrub he extends his head
and neck in front in a horizontal position, and the
vines glide from his helmet on to his shoulder and
thence harmlessly along the plumage of the back.
When scared by a sudden surprise, the bird will
rush through the undergrowth with a noise like a
scrub bullock, tearing vines and breaking bushes,
but if he has sighted you quietly he will vanish
through the densest mass of tangled vegetation in
a manner apparently miraculous. He is a much
heavier and far more powerful bird than an emu,
and is dangerous to approach when wounded or
“bailed up.” One of the three toe-nails reaches
a length of over four inches, and I have seen a
cassowary tear the side out of a dog and disem-
bowel the animal with one kick. When two males
fight they spar like boxers and kick like lightning
in three directions —sideways, straight in front,
and out behind. I have seen two large birds
strike each other simultaneously, the recoil throw-
ing both on their backs.
At night they camp between the flanges at the
root of a tree, or stand, or crouch on the breast,
in a patch of lawyer vines or undergrowth. If
approached by a man with a bull’s-eye lantern
they will not attempt to run, and can be lassooed
or knocked down.
A full-grown cassowary has no fear of a dingo
or a tame dog. The first interview of a tame dog,
especially a conceited dog, with a cassowary, is a
comical spectacle. The dog suddenly sniffs the
unusual trail, makes a wild rush forward, catches
sight of a six-feet cassowary quietly picking up
pencil cedar plums, gives a sort of canine
“ Hooray ! here’s something grand 1” and ad-
vances to the charge with all the supercilious
arrogance which contemptuously under-estimates
the resources of an adversary. The next scene
shows an astonished and terror-stricken dog, with
no visible tail, flying for his life in a cloud of dead
leaves, pursued by a cassowary with his plumes
all standing- erect and a voice that sounds—to the
dog—like a fog-horn blown by Lucifer on the
shores of Acheron. When the cassowary is out
of sight the dog sneaks back to his master's heels
and meekly enquires, in the plain language of
looks, “ Did you see that red-headed earthquake ?”
The hen cassowary lays from two to six eggs on
the bare ground, usually in some secluded part of
the scrub, in a thicket of lawyers or stinging-tree,
and she and the male divide the responsibilities of
incubation, sitting alternately on the eggs for
about five weeks. After hatching, the young
birds are placed entirely under the control of the
male bird, and I have never seen the female in
charge of her family.
In three or four days the young birds are strong
enough to run at a considerable pace, and when
danger is announced they hide under logs, dive
into holes at the roots of trees, or squat in a heap
of leaves, and lie perfectly still until the old bird
calls them together. Up to two years of age the
plumage is grey, and thenceforth it darkens
to a bright black color at five years. Maturity
is attained in seven years. When out on the
ranges, on the north branch of the Mulgrave
River, in 1885, I saw a very large and beautiful
white cassowary, evidently an albino. He had no
wattles, nor any of the usual gorgeous colors on
the neck. This noble bird walked leisurely across
the dry bed of a watercourse, and vanished in the
dense brush.
The voice of the young birds is a plaintive
whistle; that of the grown bird varies from a deep
boom to a harsh hiss. He adopts this tone when
chasing a dog.
Cassowaries are very fond of bathing, and I
have frequently seen them plunging and fluttering
their plumes, and throwing somersaults in shallow
pools. Occasionally they are attacked and killed
by crocodiles. They are frequently caught and
drowned in the valleys by the sudden floods in the
creeks and rivers. Many were drowned on the
Mulgrave River in the floods of 1890. Several
bodies of large cassowaries were found after the
waters subsided.
On the Johnstone, Russell, Mulgrave, and Bar-
ron rivers, the cassowary is known to the blacks as
“Boondarra,” “Keendadja,” “Bomba,” “Boom-
boom,” and “Goombijan.” The bird is eaten in all
stages, from theeggstothe full-grown specimen. In
the tribe of “ Bolambi,” on the Mulgrave River, I
found one class had the cassowary as a totem, and,
of course, was prohibited from eating the egg or
the flesh. The class was named “Keendadja,”
after the bird.
The New Guinea natives make daggers and
butt-ends from the leg bones, and use the plumage
for decorative purposes. The North Queensland
natives rarely use the plumage, and make no use
of the bones. The cassowary accumulates an
immense amount of oil, especially from November
to March, and I have obtained six or seven quarts
from one bird. The skin is covered by fat and
very difficult to preserve. The oil rather increases
than prevents rust on articles of steel. It is
extremely effective on stiff joints or contracted
muscles.
Professor Owen regarded the cassowary as the
nearest living representative of the ancient Palap-
tei’yx, or Dinornis, a genus of extinct gigantic
birds which included the moa of New Zealand.
The present living representatives of the once
mighty order of Ratitce , are the African ostrich,
the South American rhea, the Australian emu,
the New Zealand apteryx, and the cassowary.
Two species of Dromaius (D. patricius and D.
gracilipes), contemporary with the Diprotoodon,
have been found on the Darling Downs, and
named by Mr. De Vis, of the Q. Museum, but no
fossil cassowary remains have been recognised in
Australia.—A. Meston, in a paper before the Q.
Royal Society.
A private letter from Fort Salisbury,
Mashonaland, mentions the interesting fact that
the parsons during the late Matabele slaughter
were by far the best butchers.

The Norwegian Parliament doesn’t applaud
or make any kind of demonstration. In 80 years
there has been only one outbreak in that serious
assemblage, and that was when a Radical member
was so much carried away by his feelings as to say
“Bravo!” He was suppressed immediately, and
the outrage has never been repeated.

Faithfull Begg’s scheme for the formation of a
n Stock Conversion Trust” is apparently progressing.
At latest advices the Trust was nearly in order, and it
should shortly get to work on its contract of buying up
£2,000,000 worth of reconstructed deposits. Taking the
average prices in the various banks to be operated on—
the Victorian, National, City of Melbourne, A.J.S., Com-
mercial of Australia, London, and Q.N.B. —the lot should
be procured at 16s. 6d. all round, making a total loss to
the depositors of £350,000 when the final settlement
comes. Also, during the currency of the deposits some-
thing like £3,000,000 in interest, much of which would
otherwise have remained in Australia, will go to
England. The Stock Conversion Trust is not a very
lar”e affair, but still it is one more example of the
awful tendency of Australian money to go to England on
the smallest provocation and stay there. England is the
whirlpool which ultimately absorbs everything, and it
takes in t 1,000,000, or three-halfpence in copper, with
equal avidity. It is like the elephant which roots out and
carries away trees, or picks up a solitary pin with the
same calm acquisitiveness. Nothing is too big for it and
nothing is too small. It will grab a province on the land-
grant-railway principle with one hand, and it will take a
nigger’s cocoa-nut with the other, and it holds them
eoually fast when it has them.

The Maoriland Government, which, a few days
&go, formally announced that the amalgamation of the
B.N.Z. and the Colonial Bank was practically arranged,
has now decided to prohibit said amalgamation alto-
gether. This either means that the Cabinet doesn’t
know its own mind, or that some new trouble has arisen
which is being kept dark. If no explanation is offered it
is an unfortunate “ ad.” for both banks.
* * * *
Creditors of busted banks may take a little
melancholy interest in the fact that a compounding share-
holder who can't pay his calls is left to arrange matters
with the bank’s solicitors. He makes an affidavit and the
bank signs an agreement, the cash result being—as shown
in a recent case—£l2 10s. for the insolvent financial insti-
tution, and “£4 4s. solicitors’ costs.” Sometimes, of
course, there is much more than £l2 10s. for the institu-
tion, but, on the other hand, if the unhappy shareholder
cannot raise more than a fiver the balance handed over by
the legal firm is exactly 16s. Which reminds The Bulle-
tin again that Matthew’ Davies should be doing a good
business in drawing up these little agreements between
his busted banks and their compromising shareholders.
Matthew, however, may be one of the more reasonable
practitioners who charge only £2 2s. for putting the for-
malities through. Meanwhile a learned Judge has, with
no consideration for the wants of his fraternity, “ sug-
gested that liquidators might make some less oppressive
arrangement w'ith solicitors for lower charges, or might
themselves furnish the necessary forms.” Unfortunately,
there are not many liquidators w ho desire to deprive their
legal pals of a job.
• • * •
N.S.W. Auditor-General’s report toi Deo. 31,
’93, came out last week. Q. Auditor-General’s report
to June 30, ’94, was published more than three weeks
before. Financial criticism, to be useful, must be fresh:
and Treas. Reid might very well unravel some of the red
tape which clogs the wheels of his department.
The tardy report is a vast and complicated col-
lection of figures, with so many knots and tangles, sus-
pense accounts and reserve accounts, that a special
education is required to make definite head or tail of
them. The Auditor-General himself is not at all sure of
his whereabouts in the fog. Instead, for example, of
saying that the cash overdraft on consolidated revenue
account at Deo. 31 was £2,182,1.86, he says it “ appears to
have been ” £2,182,186. What comment w'ould a board
of bank directors make upon an account which showed a
customer “ apparently ” indebted to that tune ?
It doesn’t seem to be the Auditor-General’s
fault—his broom is powerless to sweep the Treasury
stable. His report is largely made up of details of con-
flicts with the Treasury officials, who manipulate the
public funds at their own sweet will in defiance of
Act of Parliament. Then the Auditor-General “dis-
allows” these proceedings—which last year involved
nearly £9o,ooo—and the Treasury officials practically tell
him to go and sit on the Act of Parliament. Then he
appeals to the Treasurer himself, and that intelligent
personage winders why on earth he can’t let things slide
and certify that the balance-sheet corresponds with the
books, like any other sensible auditor. So the Treasurer
hacks up his officials, and the Auditor-General sits on his
Act of Parliament, and weeps, and incorporates the cor-
resp mdenoe in his report, at which impotent revenge the
Tite Barnacles laugh consumedly. And things go on in
the same old groove.

• • * *
A Melb. bank-clerk, whose duns form a couple
of never-ending processions to and from his office, has hit
upon an ingenious scheme to choke off the obnoxious
beggahs. The bank messenger is a tall, dignified, grey-
haired individual, and, as per arrangement, whenever he
sees a suspicious-looking person in conversation with the
aforesaid clerk he walks up, with an authoritative air,
and demands—“ Have you no work to do, Mr. Jones? Go
back to your desk at once, sir. You must find some other
time to converse with your friends than during banking-
hours." Then the unfortunate tradesman melts silently
down the staircase.

Manganese is largely used at Mb. Morgan in
the production of chlorine gas. Abundance of this
mineral exists in the district south of Rockhampton,
especially in the neighbourhood of Gladstone. Yet Glad-
stone sees a vessel sail up to its own wharves laden with
manganese from Samarang, Java. Asiatic cheap labor
again and Freetrade.
* • • •
One Melbourne Yankee did a big trade last
summer with confectioners, &0., in mechanical revolving
fly-scaring machines. They turned out all right, but the
flies got used to it—not agitation enough, apparently.
Just now another Yank brings out an improved revolving
affair, which carries 54 self-acting revolving sails or fans,
and, in offering his invention for sale, describes the old
one as simply “a luxury for flies and if strong enough a
boon to youngsters, both flies and youngsters being able
to get an easy and comfortable merry-go-round all day
for nixey.”

to the end of ’93 was nearly £97,000,000. Gold, £39,853,962;
coal, £28,443,151 ; silver, £16,692,435 ; copper and tin
combined, £5,954,771.

Country storekeeper (reading newspaper account
of latest Coolgardie find): “ ‘ Prom scwt. of quartz they
obtained half-a-ton of pure gold.' I don’t see as ’ow
that’s possible.” His new-chum assistant (who feels
that unless he can give a satisfactory answer his reputa-
tion is gone): “Oh, that’s quite right, don’t you know ;
they do that by specific gravity.” Country storekeeper:
“ Well, if you ’adn’t told me, I should ’ave said it was a
blanky lie.”
* * * ♦
The Robinson Gold-mining Co., Transvaal, says
the Mining and Engineering Journal, has the distinction
of operating the largest gold-mine in the world, if actual
gold-output be taken as a standard. The Treadwell
mine in Alaska is worked on a much larger scale and
produces a greater amount of ore, its last report showing
237,235 tons worked by 240 stamps, while the Robinson
last year ran only 60 stamps and crushed 94,842 tons of
ore. The exceedingly low grade of the Treadwell ore,
which produced a little less than $3 per ton, contrasts
with an average of about $17.50 per ton for the Robinson,
giving the latter the first place as a gold-producer. The
Robinson last year derived about 79 per cent, of
its production from the ore worked in the mill, 8 per
cent, from concentrates by chlorination, and 13 per
cent, from the re-working of tailings by the cyanide
process. The latter yielded a fair profit, though the
extraction was only 68.7 per cent, of the assay
value, a proportion which the company hopes to
increase. In this mine, which has the deepest workings
on the Witwatersrand, the proportion of pyritic to
free-milling ore is increasing very rapidly with depth,
and the report anticipates an early date when the free-
milling ore will entirely disappear. This can hardly be a
pleasant outlook for those Witwatersrand companies
which have based their expectations on the main reef of
which so much has been said. The Robinson is providing
for the future, and expects to extend the concentrating
plant and the use of the chlorination process, as well as
that of cyanide on the mill tailings.
« * * •
English investments in Indian gold mining have
had varying results, but the shareholders of the leading
companies in Mysore, now the chief producing region,
have no reason for complaint. The Mysore Co. paid 50
per cent. div. last year, the same rate in 1892, 65 in ’9l,
and 75 per cent, in ’9O. The Nundydroog spent much
money last year in development and paid only 12J per
cent., but in 1892 it paid 28J, in 1891 25, and in 1890 19J
per cent. Holders of Ooregum ordinary have received in
four years 52J, 37J, 174, and 5 per cent., and preference
shares 62J, 474 i 27J-, and 15 per cent., 1893 thus showing
extraordinary results. The Champion Reef Co. paid only
10 per cent., but it paid off £50,000 outstanding deben-
tures. The Indian goldfields have not yet been fully
developed, and while there is no reason to expect any
extensive discoveries, it is possible that the production
may be considerably increased.

Dear Bulletin, —“ Xenos,” writing on a potato-
starch factory, mentions the price of potatoes at £llos.
per ton. He cannot have any idea of the average price of
that commodity. During the last five years potatoes
have averaged about £2 15s. per ton. —Spud.

Bendigo men’s enterprise shows itself at Oool-
gardie in the way they have handled the Bendigo Cool-
gardie mine. Several shafts have been sunk, one solely
for water, and a fairly good supply has been struck. A
dam has been built, and a 15-head battery is being
erected. The lodes are big and look well on the surface,
but are now too poor tor divs. The only hope lies in
striking a more permanent shoot like that of the adjoin-
ing mine, the new Victoria.

Dear Bulletin,— .Be tight places. How
do the following particulars of the recent killing of
old Bill Baird strike you ? Bill was well-known
in Cape York Peninsula as an explorer and pros-
Eector, and became celebrated chiefly through
is ownership of the best claim at Mount Romeo
tin-mines, where he made <£sooo. Unhappily,
Baird’s heart was better than his head, and when
he invested his earnings in a pub. his many
friends flocked round, and, what with lending
them money and joining in their carouses, the
cash soon vanished, and the old man had to take
to the bush once more. His last try for a recovery
was made about four months ago, on the Archer
River, Aided by two mates, he was digging a
trench when the niggers came on them with
spears, and one of them pinned Baird to the
bank. One man got speared right through the
body close to the heart, but, being a darkey, he
recovered, and, according to latest accounts, is
walking about Cooktown quite well. The
other man received a spear in the thigh. These
two got to their humpy about 200 yards distant,
stuck there for five days, and then started off to
the Mein telegraph station. Baird’s decomposed
body was found a week later by the Coen police in
the same position. He had succeeded in pulling
two spears out of his body and in taking off his
boots, so that he must have lived some time.
Just imagine the sufferings that man endured
roasting in the ground under the tropical sun, too
weak to get out, and without a drop of water to
quench his raging thirst! Add to this the mental
torture of the thought that the niggers would
return and mangle him, as is their custom, and
you have a situation that should |be well up on
your list of close fits. —B.D.S.
HOW A LOT OF MONEY LEAKS OUT.
What does a man do when he finds a hole in the
pocket where he carries his money? Anybody
can answer that question. He has it sewed up, of
course, directly—and good and strong, too. I
suppose it is with you just as it is with me. When
I spend money, even foolishly, I can tell where it
went, and maybe I’ve had some sort of pleasure
out of it. But Ido mortally hate to lose money;
lose it out and out, you know, and have no satis-
faction _ from it, or know how or when it left my
possession.
Well, now, let me show you the worst and biggest
hole any man ever had in his pocket; a hole
that lets the cash leak away like water through a
sieve, a hole that is the hardest in the world to
sew up. A short story will show it best.
“Drummore South Cottage,
“ Musselburgh, near Edinburgh,
“ September 16th, 1891.
“ Gentlemen, —Up to 1885 I was always strong
and healthy. About this time I began to feel bad.
I was tired, languid, dull, and listless, and every-
thing was a burden to me. I had no desire for
company, and what had come over me I could not
make out. My tongue and mouth were dry, and
I had a deal of phlegm on my stomach. The
whites of my eyes next became discolored and my
skin was yellow. I had no appetite, and after
eating I had great pain at my chest and sides, also
across my stomach. After a time the pain settled
in my left side, and my heart would beat and
jump in a manner that alarmed me. By-and-by I
got so weak that I was not able to go about the
house, and I felt that I ought to be in bed. The
pains at my side and stomach became so bad that
I had to remove my clothing (everything seemed
so tight), and I used to press my stomach and
hold my sides to try and ease the pain. Getting
worse, I saw a doctor at Musselburgh, and was
under him for three months, but his medicine
gave me no relief. After this I went to a clever
doctor at Preston Pans who said I was suffering
from indigestion and dyspepsia. He sent to
London for some celebrated medicine which was
packed in small phials. This medicine seemed to
dissolve my food, and I felt easier for a time, but
I gained no strength or real benefit, and after per-
severing with his treatment for six months, I
gave it up and fell into my old state. I next
went to a doctor at Musselburgh, but all his
medicines did me no good. After this I saw
another doctor (that is a fourth doctor), but with
the same result; none of them gave me anything
that reached my complaint. I now lost all faith
in physic, for I had spent a deal of money and
taken so much medicine * that I lost all my teeth
through it ’ and was no better for it. In great
misery I lingered on month after month, always
ailing, when in August of last year (1890), my
husband called at Mr. Jack’s Drug Stores, High-
street, Fisherrow, and told him what my condition
was. Mr. Jack gave him an account of the
wonderful cures he heard of from many of his
customers that had taken a medicine called
Seigel’s Syrup, and strongly recommended him to
bring me a bottle. He did so, and I commenced
taking the Syrup, and I found some relief from
the first bottle, and by the time I had taken four
bottles I was as well and strong as ever I was in
my life, and have since kept in good health. I tell
every one what Seigel’s Syrup has done for me.
‘I never thought to get better again, and I con-
sider it has saved my life. I wish others to know
this, and if by publishing this statement it will be
the means of helping others, as it helped me, you
can use this letter as you like. Yours truly
(Signed) Jemima Watson.”
Look back to about the middle of the above
letter and again read what the writer says; “I
had spent a deal of money for medicine.” Yes,
and money she could poorly afford to spare. Ill-
ness and the expense of illness is the great hole in
the pocket that I alluded to. It costs so much,
and what does it give us in return ? Pain, weari-
ness, and misery. There is another consideration
besides. When we are ill we not only have to
bear the increased outgo, but manage to meet
larger demands out of a decreased income. Our
candle is burning at both ends. “Yes,” you sav,
“ but how can we keep from falling ill ?” You
cannot always, but in view of the fact that most
illnesses arises from indigestion and dyspepsia, a
timely use of Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup will
prevent it. A few shillings thus invested will
save pounds in money and perhaps months of
wretchedness. Think over the striking points in
Mrs. Watson’s excellent letter and you will think
the same.
